RATING: 3.5
This game is a tough one to rate. It works as written (rules are a bit foggy but the FAQ's clear that up) but as written, it's a hostile, unforgiving, math-heavy game. This isn't a typical railroad game which moves at a leisurely pace and feels a bit like multi-player solitaire. Get 4+ players and this game is nasty and strategically difficult. Hardly a smile cracks anyone's face for nearly 4 hours of play. And when it's all over, your back hurts from leaning over the map  and your mind is fatigued from going through turn after turn of excruciatingly difficult economic decisions. So is this a good or bad game? Gaming can be an enjoyable time socializing, munching and playing a game or it can be "serious face" time where bathroom breaks are infrequent and every difficult decision adds another gray hair. The question is: how do you want to spend your leisure time?

See the GLG variants which help make the game a little more forgiving and a bit more friendly. Oh, and play Volldampf if you can find a copy.
  
After several plays, my original opinions stand and my rating has even gone lower. The game simply isn't any fun. It's a great mental exercise like multi-player chess but there isn't any real railroading flavor to this game. Plus, it has the problem of "runaway leader." You can't play cooperatively to stop someone. And even more problematic, if a player starts in a bad position (maybe gets cut-off early) then its basically game over for him as he'll limp along for the rest of the game. That's a game balance problem. And then there's the wait...calculate...wait...calculate... style of gameplay. 

Honestly, if it was a choice between spending an extra half day at work or playing this game, it would be a difficult decision.

RATING: 7
Martin Wallace's best economic game.  A turn opens with an intense bidding round followed by laying track tiles, shipping cubes, earning income and paying expenses.  Plenty of opprotunity to jack your opponents.

The only real weakness in the game is the urgency has a tendancy to drop off in the middle to late game.  Players are making so much money there's no threat of financial failure.  Instead it becomes a game of who can be the richest of the rich and the mightiest of the mighty.

RATING: 10
Age of Steam is a wonderful game, and less intimidating than you might think. The game rewards careful math and long-term planning, but is very enjoyable to play even if you know you're not going to win. I hope to play many, many games of AoS in my lifetime.

In addition to the official/authorized expansion maps I own, I also have put together these internet releases:

Alpha Centauri - A New Hope: A fun 2-player map that doesn't deviate from the rules, but provides an interesting landscape on which to play. A great place to teach a new player the rules. Apparently this is the first in a series of Alpha Centauri maps that will throw in more special rules as each map comes out. A very cool idea. 

Hong Kong: not played yet.

India: not played yet.

Japan: not played yet.

Northern England: not played yet.

Scotland: This is a very enjoyable 2-player map. It is tight, so don't get crazy with the locomotive. Urbanization can be very powerful here. I would like to try it using the Reunion Island locked action mechanism, but unfortunately I don't know how it works.

Spain: not played yet.

RATING: 7
I am not a great fan of Age of Steam. There are multiple barriers which lead to this situation: first of all, the quality of the artwork is very poor. It's an important factor for me in the purchase of a game and Age of Steam is not up to my standards (I'm not into fine detailed but an overall flavour and style is very much appreciated in a game). Then, I came to this game a bit late with players who already knew it. This made my first games not that much enjoyable. Still, I've now enjoyed a first (shared) victory at that game. Finally, there is a lot of interaction in that game and it is not always easy to foresee. That added to some complexity in the games itself (calculation, auctions) makes it a difficult game to get into and a long game to play prone to some analysis paralysis. Still, I'm sure it's a game worth working on... I'm looking forward to the next edition to solve some of the issues I have with the game. I can't wait for it.

RATING: 5.5
In a way, this is a "gamer's game" par-excellence.  It's deep, edgy and precise.  You needn't bother asking Aunt Maude to play this one after tea, in fact even amongst your gaming friends only the most serious would enjoy it.

I may change my rating as I play it now, but as I see it at present, the plusses here are:
great depth, agonising decisions, cut-throat play, replayability, room for lots of different strategic thinking, subtlety.

On the negative side:
get behind early and you struggle/get ahead and you're hard to stop (in some games of this, it can be a total killer), badly written (though simple) rules, dull art work, a fairly high degree of luck for such a strategic game, some players may think there's no fun involved in it (you won't get smiles just a lot of head scratching).  And it's pretty dry and very mathematical.  The last third is rinse and repeat after setting youreself up - just ship as many goods as you can for the maximum you can make.  That might be something that burns your brain in the planning, but in terms of the spin off at the end, it's pretty dull in terms of fun.

For myself, I sort of enjoyed it the first time, but felt less good about each time I played it thereafter.  I wouldn't say that it was enjoyable as Wallace's other highly acclaimed heavy game - SoE, though it's about as deep.  But that depth is in very different ways, and whilst this is good, it's not one I then long to play again, which I do with SoE.

Having recently played Railroad Tycoon, I marginally prefer that...but I'm not big on that one either.  RT is not as pure, but it's slightly more fuhn.  Actually, I'd say the game where about 80% the same, which makes RT more "AoS midi" than "AoS lite."

RATING: 9.5
Qui si entra nell'Olimpo.
Capostipite della "serie ferroviaria" di Wallace (gli altri sono Steam e Railways of the World), è il classico capolavoro senza tempo (qualcuno dirà che si vedono gli anni che si porta appresso..). 

Age of Steam non perdona alcun errore, pena la possibilità di essere eliminati dal gioco (e questo può accadere già alle prime fasi, in seguito a scelte scellerate) e mette di fronte ad un continuo adattarsi al mutamento della mappa e alla situazione patrimoniale propria e degli avversari (grazie al particolare meccanismo dei prestiti da richiedere ESCLUSIVAMENTE prima dell'asta di inizio turno).

Scala diversamente bene a seconda della mappa scelta (ce ne sono decine) e non è adatto ai neofiti (per i quali consiglio il più "soft" Railways of the World)
E' un gioco essenzialmente di pick and delivery, con una cattivissima asta iniziale e una continua sensazione di non avere tempo nè soprattutto soldi per fare quello che serve. 

Mi ripeto: un capolavoro.

RATING: 8
Enjoyed my first play at 4p. Felt perhaps a bit long, and also that there is a lot of parity and that it's difficult to make inroads on another's position until later in the game. Going first is a huge advantage that is somewhat mitigated by the special actions. The auction can be brutal to take position high in turn order. The diminishing returns from the income track seem like they will inevitably produce scores that are bunched together, as they were in our game. Also some concern about the randomness of cubes coming out (which seems to make the production bonus action close to useless unless you have literally nothing else to do). Still, a fun experience and I'm very interested to see how the plethora of expansion maps change the experience.

RATING: 8
Age of Steam is a game full of decisions on priorities, on where and how to expand, and on how much you dare spend.  Oh, and woe betide the player who does not pay close attention to what his opponents are doing. I find playing AoS an engrossing experience.  It benefits hugely from being the fourth iteration of the Early Railways system; a much longer development cycle than is usual. 

Having said all that, the game is not faultless. Management of resources in the early game is absolutely critical - to the extent that there can be no way back from a poor start. The final victory point count of track segments is really fiddly. And the rules, while better than the usual Warfrog standard, could still be better organised.

RATING: 9
Wonderful game of building connections.  Unlike many games this rewards strategic planning, i.e. sitting down at the start of the game and deciding where your deliveries of various lengths are going to come from.  This plan rarely survives completely intact, but there is often (just) enough freedom to keep it together (assuming you win the turn order auction at the right time).

Significantly better than Steam, in my opinion, as the production rules in Steam remove much of this long-term focus by making it easier to get the cubes you want when you want them.

RATING: 9.5
First play was pretty interesting; I got crushed; one player nearly doubled everybody else in score at the end. Definitely enjoying this already, interesting to see how it feels after a few more plays.

Now that I've played it a bit more, I love the game. More than that, I love how it makes a game system. The base game is great, but different maps make the game amazing without much additional expenditure.

It's loose and forgiving, compared to some other train games, but I think that's okay -- this is a nice game that rewards the skillful play, but won't leave your casual gamer friends feeling completely crushed if they screw up.

The massive quantity of additional maps prove the game out as more than just a game, but as a modular game system -- this keeps the game extremely fresh, yet people playing what feels like a whole new game need only learn 2 or 3 new rules and they're ready to go. Really can't say enough good things about the game and game system, may make it to a 10 in time...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
I've upped my rating after a few more plays. This is a tight game (the Ireland expansion makes it worse). An extra $1 bid can be the difference between winning and falling out of contention. Unlike 18xx, the game varies (based on the cube setup), but most of the variation is known ahead of time. The luck of when cubes appear means that everything is not completely known, however. Anyone liking 18xx will probably like this. Perhaps even more. I may up my rating again, once I get to 10 plays. 

[Update] Now that the expansions are out, I've upped my rating again. If you get the base game plus all three expansions, you get 7 different maps for $120. Seven different 18xx games would cost $350 (or more, for kits). This is a great game and great value.

RATING: 7
My Favorite game. I love the great balance between tactics and strategy.  Every turn you have to make sure you have goods to deliver and at the same time build your train network so that you can have longer deliveries next turn.  I love the blocking and rush for precious real estate and the clever moves all around.  The special roles add a lot to the game as people vie for the needed quirks.  The game has relatively straight forward rules for its strategic depth that new players can get into the game within a turn or two.I too, was hesitant to purchase this game because of possible player elimination but it is much a needed check to keep the feeling of someone making foolish decisions in starting a company from the ground up.  A lot of thematic complaints are really unfounded.  Income reduction is the game version of inefficiencies found in larger corporations and taxes by Uncle Sam levied on the more profitable companies.  Goods Cubes are contracts from manufacturers to deliver their products to their retailers.  Long deliveries = more income is the game version of the train company delivering passengers also in those cities and towns on the way to make good deliveries.  Why would the manufacturer care if the train company looped the country 20x if their good is delivered on time?  Like I said, this game is dripping with theme if you actually think about it.  Also take into account the impressive number of expansion maps for the game, you'll likely find one desirable.

Update: Feb-3-2010
Now that I have Steam and played it, I much prefer Steam than to AoS.  The amount of time saved by getting rid of the auction is totally worth it compared to the amount of the strategic depth it provided.  Just ranking it down due to the existence of Steam.

Age of Steam is a (very) less friendly game than Railways of the World: it is filled with agonizing decisions, and every moment in the game felt like you're walking on a tight rope. If only one of the things you planned for the turn goes wrong, you're doomed!

The answer is probably the latter, but it is easier finding opponents for the former...

RATING: 8
The appeal for some Age of Steam players is trying out the sometimes clever, sometimes not so clever changes brought on by the expansion rules/maps.  Age of Steam would probably get really stale if there were no expansions.  I now have a large collection of maps and a set of poker chips.  

My rating for Age of Steam is for the entire collection of maps and my interest in trying out new maps.  Keep in mind that the Rust Belt map would be rated lower than an 8 and certain maps would be rated much lower, such as Netherlands and Italy.  The difficulty level for a particular expansion map can vary widely depending on the number of players and the goods distribution.  

The downside is that AoS has a potential run-away-leader problem.  Unfortunately the random goods production contributes to this outcome perhaps a little more so than it should.  However, if you do find yourself out of the competition early (which I often do) you can still enjoy yourself by playing for 4th or 5th place.

RATING: 8
This game just doesn't seem to have what I seek in a game. It's not fun, it's work. Feels very constraining. Where's the freedom? Where's the fun? Unfriendly, unforgiving, unrelaxing, unexciting, and far too serious. If a game doesn't give me something to smile or laugh about, there's something wrong, and it's not me. Pass.

First two games were chuckers. I'd probably give it one more shot. It would take some heavy convincing though.

Finally played for a third time. I broke even and came in 4th out of 6! That is good for me. The game is starting to make more sense. Seems like the idea is to get locomotive up early without killing yourself in the process. You can't win without a locomotive of at least 5. So get it up to 4 as soon as possible. Watch cities for closing up of hex sides because if there is an open hex then you can probably get to it. Go for cities away from others and with few open sides. In this way monopolize the connections to them.

My rating for this game goes up and down with each play. It's been as high as 8 and as low as 3. Right now its at 5. I'm afraid if I get too geeked to play this that it will blow up in my face. I'm approaching this game with caution.

Played game #4 of this. Felt more in control than ever (had a little good luck too), but it was still a tough tough challenge. I'm definitely getting better at "seeing" the board, where the cubes are going to go, etc.

Game #5 and I feel like I've taken a step backward. But maybe it's because I taught the game and proceeded to play down to 3 newbies. I just can't seem to teach and play well in the same game. Grrr. Was a fun game though. My rating has improved from a 5 to a 6. Sucks, however, when all your plans hinge on getting the locomotive and someone outbids you for it.

RATING: 3
A very disappointing game after the massive praise bestowed upon it. Ignore the player scale, this really only works for 4-5.  With three the game loses interaction and recedes into boredom and with six the downtime exceeds tolerance.  Still, that leaves a game wholly unfulfilling and laden with blatant design problems.

First, though I can not demonstrate exactly what mars the turn order auction , it fails as an interesting mechanic.  Second, the role system feels tacked on and unbalanced.  Third, though rarely coming into play, the track-laying rules are quite complex.  Most egregious, however, is the production system.  Random goods placement?  In a heavy game like Age of Steam?  Without the Producer role the game falls into stagnation, yet that role suffers endemic weakness and will likely never appear.  A decent game is buried within, but it's not in this box.  A box full of relatively poor components for the price.

RATING: 6
Martin has moved this system ahead with the publication of "Steam" which has, arguably, smoothed off some of the rougher edges of this entry in the series. This is still a decent game but I will likely be playing "Steam" much more often now,
------
Meatier and more thematic than the lighter "crayon rails" or "Railways of the World," where the emphasis is on building and operating rail lines with several clever design hooks at work: bidding for turn order and "role" selection; financial planning that requires thought yet without the need for a calculator; track laying skill; planning goods shipment; and, maintenance management.

This game does have a degree of randomness, and can be unforgiving, particularly in the early stages, making surviving the first couple of turns an accomplishment in itself for novice players. Martin gives us a rail game that is relatively easy to learn but requires some dedication to play well.

A number of annoying "quirks" (the blind cube draw Production role, dice driven goods replenishment mechanic, need for the grey disks (sometimes) to mark towns, and the gamey income reduction device, detract from my full admiration of this game, and my game rating has gone down markedly since the publication of Steam: Rails to Riches which polishes the jagged edges of all these things.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I don't like the fact that it is possible to go bankrupt and be out of the game in turn 1. This almost happened in my first game... grrr... Also too many analysis paralysis situations in this game.

RATING: 6
Good railway game, but very very unforgiving in the 'early' game - which makes it hard for new players to play with a group of veterans. Some gamers will hate the mechanic for adding more goods cubes to the game (dice rolls).

Shine has come off a bit - at its heart this is a 'nasty' game, it rewards negative play, you want to build track to make your opponents pay more (unless it's costing you more to do it), you want to ship cubes that are in 'shared' cities etc. I find the mental calculations this game needs combined with the 'screw' factor take the fun away a bit.

** Update, enjoyment varies with the map, but I'm getting increasingly frustrated with the Auction mechanic, as it is in effect 'blind' (you don't know what your opponents want, so you have to guess) and it really hurts to be 2nd if your opponent picked the action you wanted ... just not playing it as much now.

Even put the lot up for trade.

** Update - the lot is gone now, and I don't miss it. Saying that I'd play it if someone else wanted to.

RATING: 10
The gold standard of pick-up-and-deliver games.  Players build track and deliver cubes form one city to another to earn income.  The game is brutal in several ways.  First, depending on the map, there are lots of opportunities to block other people from laying track into cities and lots of opportunities to steal cubes other players need to deliver.  Second, money is very tight and it is easy to go bankrupt early in the game.  Managing money and deciding at the beginning of the round how will be needed for the auction and then to take advantage of the special action you'll get because of the auction is challenging.  Finally, the analysis of the map and the cubes in the growth area and then applying it to decide where to build/expand/deliver is deliciously difficult.  This game knocks you down and the better you do, the more it knocks you down.  I've really enjoyed my first two plays and am looking forward to more.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Age of Steam is one of those rare games that is always tense, always inspires the flop-sweats, and burns the brain hard. At its heart, it is a pick-up-and-deliver game with route-building, but it is the auction mechanism that makes the game what it is. The auction determines turn order and a once-a-turn privilege of some type: the ability to move first, or build first, or increase your delivery range etc. Both turn order and these privileges are the key to winning, and money is incredibly tight, so the auction becomes the central dance, the real game-within-the-game.

What really makes the game great, beyond the tightness of the design, is the amount of maps for the system. Because the game is generally rules-light, individual maps add on chrome or change/augment mechanics in ways that completely rework the game without the disruption of a lot of new rules. Some maps change the available privileges, some change the costs to build, some constrain play simply with tough geographical design. There are many maps, and many new experiences, in the system, such that regular play is rewarded with new challenges that nicely don't take more than 10-15 minutes to learn.

This is by far Martin Wallace's best game, and easily deserving of its reputation. I've played at all counts, and as long as the map is appropriate, it scales well. The 2er can be lame, depending on the map, but the Alspach maps are great with 2, and it's worth playing as training for the multiplayer game.

RATING: 4
UPDATE: After finally coming to grips with this game in mid-2006 (before I was logging plays) ... I haven't actually played since. There are just too many other, much better economic/money-management type games, like Power Grid, 1825, Goa, or now Container. Heck, in all honesty, I'm more drawn to Monopoly after re-discovering it in 2007 (I am now starting the timer on the arrival of the lynch mob). None of these games have quite the mix that Age of Steam does, but all are much superior games (and yes, that includes Monopoly, played correctly and with 4). So down the rating goes.
---
Moreso than most games, Age of Steam is tough to rate. It's definitely a flawed game: the endgame (especially the bidding) is unreasonably wonky, since cash doesn't count for anything in the final scoring; the early game is too unforgiving, and it's basically impossible to recover from a bad start; the theme is basically a paste-up job; and the roles are poorly-balanced, especially (again) in the endgame, where everyone is bidding for the Train and the second-place bidder gets completely screwed. It's definitely a niche game: unforgiving, analytical, and gratuitously difficult.

For all these reasons, it took me a long time to accept Age of Steam at all. But eventually I did, and despite its problems it definitely has some charms. The economic feel - that of constantly flirting with bankruptcy and disaster - is unique. The route management is interesting if completely abstract. The variety of maps gives it an excellent range of experience (too bad the map actually included in the base game is one of the weakest). And it's in a heavier weight class that doesn't have a lot of good entries.

In this category of games, I definitely find Goa and 1825 much superior, mainly because they are more interactive, more textured, better-themed, less grindingly analytical, much better-balanced, and just more fun. But Age of Steam is a good entry too, and despite its flaws, does provide a unique gaming experience, which is worth something. This may sound like damning the game with faint praise, and maybe I am; but it seems to have just enough there to keep me playing and enjoying it a few times a year.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
This game is brutal!  One of the tightest games I've ever played, as the slightest mistake or miscalculation can royally screw you over.  Now, I love this sort of thing in a game, where not only the opponents are out to get you (and yes, they will royally screw you over), but the game itself is out to get you as well (and yes, it will royally screw you over).

The numerous maps available for this game also make it awesome.  Every map set has a couple minor rule changes, but results in a totally different puzzle to solve, requiring different strategies.  The base game is pretty replayable as it is, but add in expansion maps and this game stays fresh no matter how many times you play.

It's all done within a fairly simple rule set as well, which is great!  The base rules are simple, which allows the various maps to tweak things a bit without it being complex.

Overall, just an awesome game.  If you like games that make your head hurt a little while simultaneously kicking you in the crotch, don't pass this one up!

RATING: 7
Updated 12/3/10.

1 play.
6 players.

Thanks to the Greater Boston Guild for allowing me to crash one of their game nights this month. 

Six players is almost certainly the wrong way to learn Age of Steam, but, despite the chaos, I could tell I was in the presence of a brilliant design. I had played basic Steam once, and I recall enjoying it, but Age of Steam is a different beast altogether. I'm not exactly enamored with pick-up and deliver games, but I love tense ones, Age of Steam's turn order auction, action selection, and share mechanic are things of beauty on that front. 

There were a few drawbacks to the game. Despite my impassioned defense of Dominant Species a few months ago, I still appreciate quality, attractive components and Age of Steam was unquestionably devoid of them. (FWIW, I think we played with the first or second edition). At one point, I overlooked a great opportunity because I had mentally divorced those weird-looking mountains from the buildable area. I'm also not sure how I feel about the random goods distribution and production action, but one play is probably far too little to understand the relationship between those mechanics and gameplay.

I made some really obvious first-time blunders, but I'd love another shot.

RATING: 8
Age of Steam is a masterpiece. One of my Top Ten boardgame favourites. Tile laying, tight money management, logistics, technological advances (better locomotives) and competition for various privileges all combine into what is a challenging game without excessive rules or playtime. It's situated in the central ground between the lightweight Zug um Zug/Ticket to Ride and (super)heavyweight 18XX series.

That said, it is not my favourite Martin Wallace game (my favourite designer). I happen to like Princes of the Renaissance even better. Yet AoS is a wonderful system and new boards/expansions have added new twists to the basic game. Of those I've played, the Scandinavian and Korean maps are currently my favourites.

RATING: 5.4
I have various problems with total tactical games, especially this one.  I don't appreciate the "I screw you, so you screw me back and screw that other guy" thing.  I like to be able to do a bit more planning, and be able to predict what people are going to do.  In this game, unless you play to isolate yourself, which is rewarded, you are always in reactive mode.  I also don't like the economics either.  If I take out shares, shouldn't I be able to buy them back?  Also, I probably had to take out a loan because somebody stabbed me in the back last turn.  I will tell you that whoever sells the least amount of shares will most likely win, which means they had the least amount done to them.  I appreciate "crayon rails" games more than this, because at least the theme means something.  I mean, I don't know much about railroad infrastructure, but I am pretty sure that more than a few select rails can make it through Chicago.  This game is just tight for the sake of being tight...and that is fine, but don't make the game last 2.5 hours then.

RATING: 8
Last night I finally got a chance to play Age of Steam and it really intrigued me. I started writing my comments this morning, but decided to post as a review. A game of this depth deserves many more plays before a review, certainly. And I always envisioned my first BGG review being of a game whose nuance I’d really explored. But this game made me want to contribute my initial thoughts, for whatever they are worth, in a more public forum.

In the first minutes of the game, you are confronted with crushingly stressful decisions: How many shares? What to bid? Where to start? And it doesn’t get better from there. I admire this game. It takes a planning and logistics backbone, shakes it up by adding the instability of the action auction, and puts the whole thing under extreme pressure through a compactness of geography that ensures cruel and unusual network interactions. All this in the almost impossibly unforgiving environment of the economic framework.

Every single moment requires that you struggle. You fight for survival in the early game like a drowning man, swimming for shore. Gagging and choking but relieved, you haul yourself up on the beach only to discover that food is in short supply and you’re not the only one that made it to the island.

Inevitably, as I think about the game this morning, I compare it to Power Grid. The relentless jockeying – both spatially and for turn order. The grinding cash planning required to survive multiple phases of a turn while accomplishing all that you need to, given the turn order and needs and opportunities of other players. The sense that there is always someone ready to ‘steal’ what is ‘yours’. We’ll see what happens with more plays, but I believe AoS wins this head-to-head. Spatially, the tactical possibilities seem richer. The asymmetrical needs of players in the action auction of AoS seem to generate more exciting situations than the ‘laggard leads’ method of PG. Cash planning in AoS seems much more unstable – and thus interesting – because of the degree to which other players decisions impact your plans (versus the ‘shopping list’ feeling I get a lot from PG). One area where I think PG wins out is in the players’ control of game length and work it to your advantage. Moreover, this variable game length gives each play its own palpable pace and character.

As much as I admire the game, however, I realize that it may never be a favorite. I’ll give it more time, certainly. And, at least for the near future, I won’t turn down a game. But ultimately, I think this game is too much of an exercise in competitive optimization for it to really shine for me. As such, it seems to be at or near best-in-class. But the heavier games that I have been drawn to (or maybe just dream about) have an emergent brilliance, a strategic ebb and flow that permeates the game, that allows leeway for artistry, for the ‘beautiful game’. So, I’ll end with a question to those that have played more than I: Played by experienced players, can a session of AoS be a work of art?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Excellent economic/train game.  My first game lasted about four hours, but it was exciting from start to finish. RATING: 10
Wallace's crown jewel. Takes the railroad track building and delivery mechanics, and boils them down into a manageable 2 hour screw-a-thon. Viciously tight money-wise and has a ton of long term planning, occasional auction intrigue, and just works well with its challenging economic system. The first few turns are critical, and having a vision (with back up plans in mind) seems to be the way to go. I was originally concerned that variability of the game might hurt it in the long run, but the burgeoning horde of alternate boards and random cube set-ups substantially change things up so this has not become an issue.

The reason I love this game is because of its tightness. One starts the game in debt, and the entire early sequence is dominated by the desperate need to pull out of the red and back into the black. Depending on the board and the goods distribution this can either be a matter of time (given solid play) or excruciatingly challenging. The need to plan ahead also appeals to me, particularly the pre-auction/round share issuing scheme which makes players really sit down and evaluate where they are and where they want to get to prior to doing anything else in the round.

If there are quibbles here, it is mainly with the initially fiddly rules. The upside is that the fiddly rules *are* there for a reason, balance. Trying to get people used to ideas like non-around the table in clockwise order auctions can take a bit of work, but the payoff is high, and most definitely worth the effort. I find that the towns also can be difficult for new players to fully grok, though again, I also consider them crucial because of the way they affect track building.

Age of Steam, since I first picked it up, has become a lifestyle game for me. My core gaming group loves this as much as I do, and we play it every time that we meet. My secondary gaming groups are also fond of it, and so I also play it with them. There are just so many available expansions for the game now that even playing this game every week, even multiple times in a single day, has not managed to burn me out on it.

This is the king of heavy weight 'pick up and deliver' games, and is highly recommended for people who like their games longer with a hint of sizzled brain tissue in the air.

(Note: the rating on this entry reflects my overall feelings on the game in general whereas my ratings of the expansions reflects them relative to one-another. The Rust Belt is hard to rate because it feels so basic compared to some of the new ideas, and it would likely deserve a 6 on its own merits) which it receives for being solid gameplay wise, but unspectacular in execution.)

RATING: 7
Though this game predates Steam, I have less experience with it and so tend to focus my comparison in that direction.

[b]Comparison to Steam[/b]
At first my main fear was that the randomness in AoS would detract significantly from the game. This is not really the case, since most of the resources that actually enter the game are on the production chart at the outset. The only real randomness is the timing, and given all the other decisions that a player has to make it should rarely be noticeable.

Upon playing, I found out my *real* criticism lay in a different direction. The notion of limiting the number of loans that a player may take is a good one, but the number is much much too high. Players have to be fairly incompetent not to build up a positive income fairly early, and so with 7-8 turns players shouldn't be allowed to take more than 7 or 8 loans (probably lower to be effective). 

On a similar note, I rather like the VP vs. Points decisions in Steam. Having only one track is a bit odd, although the `income reduction step' is wonderful (perhaps not punishing enough though).

As expected, the difference in delivery rules goes in favor of AoS. Although I haven't seen it come up, since players usually do best in this sort of game by scoring max points vs. the field. I have seen a number of situations in Steam where it would have been quite nice.

([i] June 2010, we played Steam with the original AoS delivery rules ::thumb::. It not only makes it easier to teach, but is a better way of playing.[/i])

Unexpectedly, the tile set for AoS is more brutal/limited ( :D ) than in Steam. Unfortunately the prices for laying track or upgrading is more difficult to keep in mind as it is not quite as uniform as in Steam. Also the points scored for track at the end game are stupidly done (per hex each link rather than per link).

Overall, the system is less forgiving of error. You will need a high income to deal with the loans you've saddled yourself with early in the game. This in turn means that you will be suffering income reduction for the bulk of the game. In Steam your economy essentially pays off your loans early, and then you shift to a focus on points. In AoS your focus is to get up and running as efficiently as possible. In AoS it is easy to inconvenience your opponents' track lays due to the restricted tile set, thus lending more importance to the turn order auction. In Steam your focus is on building up long runs to make late in the game, you will almost certainly have a 6-locomotive rating. In AoS your focus is on making sure your opponents have to give you points on their long runs because you will probably NOT have a high locomotive rating.

[i]Surprise Verdict[/i]
The perfect information, no randomness, version of the game (Steam) loses out. Age of Steam manages to be the better game. Though there is a better game out there still to be had. I see no reason that the Production and Urbanization actions in Steam can't be ported backwards and played with the AoS game components and core rules.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
So far, I have only played the game with 2 players. Once you get over the feeling that this game really, really doesn't like you, it is fun to play. In fact, the most important realisation is that the game really doesn't care whether you ever break even or not before it ends; it merely compares relative performance to other players. The only [i]real[/i] threat facing players is ending up with no cash and a maximum amount of issued stock, but most maps are so friendly that you really have to make an effort to make this event occur. There are maps designated in the correct AoS jargon as [i]tight[/i] where you do have be on your guard, though. 

[b]Update[/b] Finally, I've been able to play the game with a larger number of people. There is more contention for resources, and that makes it more interesting as a whole. There is competition for turn order, and for prime railroad expansion. Overall, the game is a nice one, if a bit long and boring for the final 25% or so (on the Rustbelt map, that is). The problem as I see it is that at some point people will obtain sufficient money from moving goods to cover for their expenses [i]and[/i] still have some cash left for extension for their railroad imperium; and from that moment on it's just semi-automatic incoming hoarding, and trying to squeeze as much points out of track laying as possible. Income is far better, though. I also noticed that a lucky dice throw can really make or break your game, meaning there's a bit of kingmaking near the end. That doesn't sit quite right with me: Railroad Tycoon simply does away with replenishing alltogether, and that might be preferable even if it means that railroads become quite useless.

I think Age of Steam is a nice game, certainly one of Martin's better and more accessible ones, even if John Bohrer did most of the development—perhaps that's the reason why it plays better than the average Wallace. All I need now are a few more maps to cater for different amounts of players, and I'm all set for the future. I'm not going to revise the rating, although if you want to, you can read the 7 as a '7+' or even '7++'. The base game is a bit of a slow starter and lacks good options and tension near the end. Different maps may alter this, of course.

[b]Update 2[/b] I've since played the newer edition of the core mechanics Steam, and concluded that to me the way Steam handles points and shares, and goods replenishment, is clearly superior and more playable. (Yes, I am aware that some game scope is lost in return.) See my comment for Steam for more information. As a result, I've downgraded my rating for Age of Steam by 1 point to reflect my updated opinion.

[b]Update 3[/b] I auctioned off this game, and will now begin to look for a replacement copy of Steam.

RATING: 6
I like this game but it is teensy-weensy brutal and thus decided to give it a 6.

I like games that are fair and designed so that players of all skill levels have a chance at victory (even if this chance is much higher for the more experienced players). This game fails at that. I've had games were people who knew the rules and had playd before lost after round 1 because the game makes it possible to sabotage them so badly that they go bankrupt and get tossed out of the game. Do we make them sit on the side for two hours while the rest of our group finishes the game or do we just restart everything? Usually it's the latter. This restarting is not fun for anyone. (ok ok, I admit, I did smile when I caused the bankruptcy on purpose).

In this game you need to calculate a lot and understand where to take risks which can possibly hurt you but still not make you bankrupt if the worst comes. Doing these calculations can sometimes take long which tends to drag the game. Thus the game is not very smooth at times. Also, some of the possible actions are just so much more better than others that whoever wins the action auction naturally picks those. That is a bit boring.

The components are ok while not anything striking. There are better games out there with the same-ish theme and mechanics.

Truly heavy brain burning game. This is Railroad Tycoon on steroids. A great game about train and delivering goods. It needs heavy foreplanning, and very unforgiving game. You could be entered into death spiral if you are not careful. 

The great things about this game is how you plan your move and track building, into the last turn of delivering maximum length of route. The production part of the game really handled well by some luck, might affect your strategy if you are not prepared.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Age of Steam is a mixed bag. Its tight focus on income and debt management through board control creates a series of interestingly agonizing decisions. Players will often have to choose between addressing virtually equal priorities, with disastrous results for incorrect or suboptimal decisions.

But at the same time, the game also feels like work; it feels like the system itself, and not the other plays, is the prime antagonist. There are huge disparities in the usefulness of available actions, particularly the overwhelming importance of the locomotive action, which makes the auction relatively transparent and highly dependent on positional elements. Since actual cash counts for nothing during scoring, the auction also becomes less interesting in the endgame, deflating the arc of the game. In addition, the game features a counterintuitive income system that rewards the most convoluted track placement.

But most frustratingly of all, it most definitely drags on for longer than it warrants. For instance, in many cases the first round’s outcomes carry such significance that players will effectively be eliminated from contention on crowded boards, and know it, forcing them to play against the game system to merely keep from going bankrupt. This is not necessarily a bad thing in a tightly designed economic game, if the length is right - but it is a particularly frustrating occurrence with three more hours to go, much of it downtime. End scoring also features one exception to the simplicity of the rules; counting track markers is fiddly and of marginal use in a game when players generally build the same amounts of track in the course of each game, and adds to the length.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Components: Sturdy pieces, clear symbols, and interesting board layout. The graphic design is a bit drab and Armyish, but the game is certainly functional.

Experience: There are several interesting elements to this game, such as trying to build inefficient networks, and despite the game's complexity it is fairly sleek; really the only fiddley part is the special powers toward the beginning of each phase.

I recognize this as a game that rewards careful play, and I think I see what others enjoy about it, but it runs long for my tastes, and I'm becoming turned off from the point-maximization mechanism found in this and other games. La Strada captures all the elements of Age of Steam that I like, but without the length and without a few added layers of complexity; I would choose to play La Strada rather than this title again.

Last Played: 2005-02-05
Eagerness to Play Again: Eventually

RATING: 10
A hard outer shell with a chewy center. Some players won't get past the 3+ hour play time, the random goods distribution, the mildly confusing board layout (color-matched track tiles would have been nice), or even the basic turn structure, which can be a bit hard to remember (player aid cheat sheets are a must for the first game). Once you do get past those issues you find a very meaty game; competitive bidding, resource management, excruciating decision-making, and varied strategies. It has a steep learning curve, but boy is it worth it. This truly is one of the best boardgames that I have played.

RATING: 4
I recognize that this is a very good game, however, I have some problems with it.

1. Track scoring: All the way through the idea is to go for efficiency; getting the most bang for buck out of all your limited number of actions, including laying track. 

Then at the end you get more points for having built inefficiently, for having built more track. You even get points for building unusable tracks that go off to empty towns. 

Why is that? The last turn of looking for a build that has nothing to do with your rail network or the goal of the game, delivering goods to cities, seems particularly tacked on. (The same stupid last turn exists in Brass and Age of Industry.)

2. Why the fixed manifest of track types? Yes, I know there's only so many tiles they could shove in the box, but thematically how is it possible to build a certain type of complex connection and then not be able to do it again? Was the technology lost to humanity? It's part of the general constraining atmosphere of the game. There are so many things you want to do that you can't do. No reason, you just can't.

Normally I like heavy games. I like to dig in and figure the darn thing out. But for some reason I sit there looking at AoS thinking, "Oh, who gives a f@#*?" Maybe it's the fact that even if I do figure it out either someone will wreck my plan accidentally or it will start to work too well and someone will wreck it on purpose.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
While other games can be filled with tension, none embodies tension in my mind like Age of Steam. Almost every moment of every game is filled with this impending feeling of doom as you stand on the edge of a cliff, where a single wrong step can send you hurtling to your doom as other players rush past you into their ultimate victory. 

The reason for this tension is the game's tightness. The game feels like it is actively working against you, and any action the players take that can disrupt your plans can push you off the metaphorical cliff. It is possible to make it so other player's plans are less disruptive to yours (by taking more shares), but in doing so you reduce your odds of victory, as each extra share taken reduces your victory point total by 3 at the end of the game. 

Age of Steam is a largely strategic affair, and it is typical for experienced players to spend a few minutes before the game starts mapping out what the general structure of your track should look like by the end of the game. But this planning is based on an ideal state of affairs, and the actions of other players are usually sufficient to force you to desperately revise your plans in mid-play forcing you to try to juryrig something together that won't result in your loss. It can be beautiful to behold.

RATING: 5
There's a lot to think about in this game - money management, building track, moving goods, map development.   So, in that sense, there is a lot happening and it makes the game pretty involving and intense.  That's an important point, because there is a huge amount of downtime in this game, and it can be very unengaging unless your brain is on overdrive.  The play itself is awfully mathematical and quite repetitive.   The network building is abstract enough that it doesn't really feel like a "train" game at all, it's more like circuit building.   It's also counter-intuitive in that long circuitous routes are better than short efficient routes.  It's well known that the game begins on a tightrope and you can be knocked out in the first couple of turns if you overspend by even $1.   The system works well enough, but I'm surprised that people are so passionate about the map expansions.     I suppose that really gives the game it's life and longevity, however.    Enjoyed it well enough, and would play again, but I'm not blown away - it's cold, mechanical and ugly.   

After 5 years of play, I enjoyed this more than I expected to.  The fact that player colors are the same as the cubes bothered me more this time.  The cold and mechanical elements bothered me less.   I'm pretty sure that I like Steam a bit better than this.

RATING: 4.25
AoS has a great core system, but the game built around it is just too punishingly difficult to enjoy.  There are so many artificial restrictions: when you can take shares, or upgrade your engine, or whatever else you want to do.  Not only that, but there are always at least 2 players wanting to do the same thing, so you have to slit your own wrists in an auction just to get start player or the right to do the thing you need.  Even if you're finally riding high and making money, the game keeps pulling your profitability back.  
Delivering cubes is fine, but I'd rather play one of the friendlier and less-irritating revisions of this than the original.

RATING: 8
Age of Steam comes with a game board  representing Eastern USA. Today, there are many expansions available to the public due to it's popularity over the years since it came out. Even some  expansions are made by real Age of Steam fans!

I enjoy the Eastern USA map with 5 or 6 players the most. I find that with less players, it's less of a challenge game wise (not to be confused with less fun). 
Since there is lots more room on the map with less players to build your railroad network and less goods that being moved each turn, there is less confrontation between players railroad networks for certain spot and goods.

It's very tight on the map and it's more aggressive with 5 and 6 players. Specially where move goods is concerned. It can be very important. Sometimes you could lose a game with a certain good is taken before your turn when deliveries are tight. Have the first build can also be a huge factor in some turns.

Now a days I rarely the basic map. Expansions are very interesting for some and is worth a look.

Down graded Age of Steam a 1 point. Rarely play it in Montreal. Play it most often in Quebec City and at the WBC. Doesn't mean it's a bad game, just a lack of interest with Montreal gamers.

Traded one copy.

RATING: 8
This is a great game that never got traction with my group (though I’m holding onto a copy with hopes that I still might be able to grow a subset group of us). I used to prefer Steam but that petered out due to the simple logistical nature of it and I’ve come to appreciate the risk management exercise that AoS is. 

[q="JC’s Comment"]We can also argue if the randomness is justified, and the question is mostly irrelevant. The use of randomness in the game is a design-intent function that pushes the primary focus of the game back from any tendency it had toward strict logistical planning, back toward contingency planning and execution. In short the random production re-affirms that the central question of the game is not pick-up-and-deliver or even simple execution efficiency but multiply fraught logistical planning. It is not a game of logistical cube movement, it is a game of continuously managing risk on punishingly thin margins -- and if that's not what you're looking for, then AoS isn't the right game for you.[/q]
[q="JC's Insta-Production Variant"]Upon each cube delivery, the active player takes the top cube from the production chart for either the source or destination city and places it on the matching location on the map. If the active player selects an end of the route that has no more cubes left on the production chart, then no cube is produced on the map. Instead a random cube is drawn and put in the relevant empty column on the production chart. In the special case that both columns on the production chart are empty, the new cube is drawn first and the active player decides in which column to place it second.[/q]
[q="Juho's Comment on the economies of AoS vs Steam]Income reduction makes the cost-benefit calculation of taking an extra loan much harder. The loan cap acts as a way of forcing players to profitability at some point, rather than allow them to indiscriminately borrow more and more while below break-even. Because you'll probably run out of runway before that. On some of the tighter maps (e.g. NorCal, London) that struggle to profitability alone can be exhilarating. [/q]

RATING: 9
This rating is for the game as a system. The game rules are tight, it's fairly easy to learn, but makes for an incredible play experience. Every move is a tough decision and the variety of maps available, each with their own set of rules makes this a game with A long shelf life. One of my favorites as of today.
If you are looking to pick up this game, look to get the treefrog edition. The plastic trains from the eagle edition really are too large and clutter the board somewhat. 15mm discs can be purchased online from several retailers, but it will generally cost between $18 and $25 after shipping.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Excellent and challenging game where a single early mistake can annul any brilliant plan one could have.  The first 3-4 turns are spent reaching an equilibrium between income and expenses; then ideas of profit can be entertained.  Since money is the focus of most of the early concerns, it seems odd that in the endgame the only real use is to build like mad or bid for a favorable play order position.  The different roles are interesting as their desirability varies greatly from turn to turn.  Keep an eye on that goods display as it pretty much tells you what is in store in the near future.  Good tension, excellent challenge and definitely will hit the table again some time soon.  Minor issues: components are bland for a game of this price, an error on a game map is never a good sign when something that obvious is forgotten; for an avid 18xxer, the luck factor in the Goods Growth phase can be severely irritating, it might seem like a small thing, but since everything has to be painstakingly planned out, it bothers me to no end that a fortuitous roll can create instant wealth for a few lucky players.  As a major peeve, we had a hard time figuring out some details during our first game since some of the rules are not clearly written at all.  In one example of the goods movement a red cube is moved and stops in Cincinnati (which is a blue city) and the income track is adjusted, whereas the rules say that a cube must be moved to a city of its colour.  Links along the way contribute to the income growth, but to do so, the final destination has to be of the same colour.  For a game that requires such attention to detail, I find that such egregious oversights are silly at best.  Thankfully a quick trip on BGG allowed to clarify such problems and we had a really good time our first time around.  I look forward to trying it a few hundred more times.

Player Interaction: :star::star::star::nostar::nostar:
"Take That" Conflict: :halfstar::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:

I have only played a couple of games of this (along with a full game on the England map), and I find it a very engaging, rather puzzle-like game that I would jump at the chance to play with anyone willing.  But that is kind of a problem - finding someone willing.  It's definitely a gamer's game.  The look of it probably won't garner much attention from anyone else, and I find it a stretch to call it a railroad game - the analogy doesn't completely work.  But it is an awesome pick-up and deliver game system, fraught with very difficult decisions and a great auction element.  Someone - please play this with me!!!!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
[b]Design:- [/b]:star::star::star::star::star:  [i]Incremental turn structure with rule-breaking actions.[/i]
[b]Execution:- [/b]:star::star::star::star::star:  [i]An acceptable level of chaos injected into a defined structure.[/i] 
[b]Experience:- [/b]:star::star::star::star::star:  [i]A thoroughly engrossing game which leaves players suspended up to that climactic turn.[/i]
[b]Presentation:- [/b]:star::star::star::star::nostar:  [i]Graphically bland but functional.[/i]

I really didn't think this one would be as good as it was.  I was very worried at first that the rule for player elimination would upset things, but now I can see that rule is obviously there just to prevent players taking ridiculous risks and starting again without any real penalty (which is what would happen if we allowed players to do so).  With the elimination rule, players have to watch their tight capital isn't dwindled away on player turn order bids, excessively expensive track building, or careless early engine upgrades.

I expected this game to be less exciting, but it's touch and go all the way.  More plays have pushed this up to a ten for me from nine.

Age of Steam, like Power Grid, is a game enhanced greatly by expansions. While I think that I could play the base map dozens of times without boredom, the fact that hundreds of more maps exist is a real draw for me. I'm not really an expansions fanatic per se; instead the draw of Age of Steam (again like Power Grid) is that it's a non-obvious game system as evinced by its proliferation of maps. As my regular readers probably know -- I'm a total sucker for systems.

RATING: 6
I thought I'd enjoy this one more.  I'll admit, I was in last place the whole game, but I don't think that affects my rating as I could sort of put myself in the seat of the winner and get a sense of if I would enjoy it anymore. I think not. I think the people who love it make excellent points for them and the people who don't also make good points. I'm in the centre. I like economic games, with some heavy thought and intriguing mechanics. I rarely have a problem with auctions and I don't have a problem with them here. But here's the shit: I admit, I made a bad move on the second turn. I built a track where I shouldn't have and it didn't get me anywhere. Here's the problem: at that point, the second turn, I had already lost the game and was 99% certain that I'd stay in last place the whole game. Everyone concurred in the next few turns that there was nothing that I could do to build up "steam". I was forced to rack up too much debt early (or just sit around with zero dollars hoping for something random to happen with goods.) Age of Steam is absolutely punishing. To those who avoid the punishment, it rewards those who count the best and get lucky with the goods distribution. Yeah, not my type of "fun". I would never say AoS is a bad game or a broken game or anything like that. There's lots of goodness here for those who love it. But for me, with so many games, I need to be having fun. Something like Power Grid- another economic linking game- I actually smile throughout it. No so with AoS. It is a mean, unforgiving, stroke the leader sort of game, and I can do without it.

RATING: 7
Age of Steam is a game that gets the true gaming blood pumping.  The tight economy, the agonizing decision making and the strong ability to hinder others as you provide small growth for your own rail company lends Age of Steam to be one of Martin Wallace's strongest offerings.  The player interaction is medium, but it never approachs a feeling of solitaire.  My initial rating is an 8 but in all likelyhood, with more play time this one could easily rise.  Less accessible than it's younger brother Railroad Tycoon (not to mention less glitzy) yet it shows the wisdom and grace an older sibling should have by being a better all around game.

RATING: 9
[b]Age of Steam[/b] [i]Martin Wallace 2002[/i] 
A wonderful system that mixes one of the best auction mechanics in the market with a bunch of interesting and difficult decissions in the actions phase. Just a masterpiece. Its new version (Steam) is also a great game, more forgiving, but I prefer the old version over it because it is more demanding for the gamer, although its production system is less dinamic than the one in Steam. Expansions add replayability to this excellent Wallace design that is only second to Brass. Hard and demanding game that I have been enjoying it for many years and I will keep on doing, may be it is time to add an expansion map to my collection, since the Rust Belt map has been played thousand times in the last five years...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Age of Steam is unrealistic and confusing for beginners.  If you get your economy going early, you will do well.  In fact you'll probably win.  How your economy gets going is quite irrational.  In the end the scores will look close, thanks to the income-levelling of the game.  In reality, whoever gets a good early run will win.  My recent experience was of a good relaxing night tarnished by playing a board game with inane rules.  To start with you are hamstrung by your economy (you actually go backwards despite the fact that you haven't bought anything yet; also, shares that you buy can never be sold - they just cost you money forever; when other people have built railway track, you can't build there too -- we are talking about track separated by many kilometers/miles; if you use other players' track you pay them all the benefits that you would have received from using the track anyway; when one railway business buys an engine, no one else is allowed to buy one???  What? - is there one manufacturer and they only churn out one engine from their factory per turn??  So unrealistic).  When your economy gets going money counts for nothing because you have vastly more than you need and nothing to do with it than bid for turn order.  Why waste your time playing this?  Have a lemonade and talk about something productive or do some exercise.  Thanks for the introduction to this game, but there are plenty of games where you have a real chance based on skill and not on how many people steal the resources you want to use.  A big thumbs down for this game.  Not recommended.

RATING: 8
I'm torn. Age of Steam is mostly a great game -- I like most everything it does. It's very meaty and (seemingly) rewards thoughtful play (i.e., it can be a brain-burner). However, this weight is let down by the end-game where kingmaking raises its ugly head.

Players can usually choose whom they help when transporting cubes. Too often this leads to deciding who wins the game. Most multi-player games suffer this to some degree but it's very pronounced in Age of Steam and especially egregious because of its heaviness. Why bother devoting all that effort if the result is a popularity contest?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Was warned about the unforgiving nature of the game so my wife and I first played the game by taking two colours each and playing like they were teams.  Team play is actually pretty interesting.  Playing to have the most total victory points on your team is better than trying to have the colour with the most victory points, because you are less likely to sacrifice one colour to kingmake the other.

Playing two colours does three things to the game:

1. Player elimination does not kill the fun.  You still have one colour to play, and the game then becomes how well can you place among the remaining colours, even though you likely won't win.

2. You can take advantage of having colours that help one another.  You take moves where your first colour gets 2 income and your second colour gets 2 income, which you might avoid in a normal game so as to not give too much income to another colour.

3. Less blocking moves.  Tracks are likely much less criss-crossing because there is one less colour to block the best paths into a city.

The first point is likely the most significant, which is why my wife and I are most likely to play this game as a two colour each "team game"  and introduce it that way to third players.
Still, I would not hesitate to jump into a regular game with experienced players now that I have a feel for the game.

I understand why this game has so many high ratings.  For me, I'm just not a train-game fan, and other themes are more interesting to me.  It is a very solid game, and I recommend others try it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Brutally unforgiving economic, tile placement and pickup& delivery game, with a well done railway theme. Mistakes in the early turns are almost entirely unrecoverable, but make a decent start and this becomes a fascinating strategic battle to outmaneuver and outperform the other players. The first time I played, I was completely destroyed by Geoff Brown, and one of the other Warfrogs... and I was like 'Eh? WTF!!! How do I do this?? By my second game, against more normal but still pretty experienced players I was more up to speed and actually won the game. A good start is essential though.

If you have the 12 hours or more needed to learn to become more competitive, then perhaps this game could be of interest to you.  For me, there are too many better alternatives.  The lengthier 18xx games are, in my opinion, clearly better.

RATING: 8
[Initial Impression: 8]
Age of Steam is a very "thinky" game - it requires long-range planning from almost turn one.  However, those plans must be at least somewhat flexible, as there is a lot of competition for certain routes, and a winner can sometimes emerge by avoiding the most obvious areas for exploitation.  There are a number of interesting mechanics in the game, including income reduction, the auction for player turn that provides for each position, and special powers that can only be chosen by one player per turn.  Some of these feel a bit clunky, but you can see why they exist and it's interesting to see the player strategies that develop in response to some of the mechanisms (e.g., actively trying to not end a round with a score that is in the lower rungs of an income reduction section).  It's a game you won't always be in the mood for (or have time for), but that leaves you with a rewarding sense of accomplishment or of having learned something new, even when you lose.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Finally managed to teach and play THE train game for the first time. 4P took 1/2 hour to teach, despite all being RRT veterans, and 3 hours to finish. 2 players did well (~75 and ~50 VP), I did poorly (20~30 VP), and one did abysmally (~5 VP).

A sadomasochist's wet-dream come true. Do you have a can of kerosene? I would like to torch my copy of AOS right now. This was more painful than having my teeth pulled out, or giving birth (if I was a woman). A bullet to the head please. An accountant would LOVE this game, as this game is as dry as crunching numbers on a spreadsheet. Am I running a railroad empire, or am I constantly bailing out what is an already sinking ship? Where is the joy in that?

Although we were all RRT veterans, I think we've been spoiled by the rather forgiving nature of RRT (except when one is dumb enough to issue 25+ shares of stock--I've done it, it's not fun). RRT is expansive, the board is huge, so you really feel like you're building a railroad empire, sort of like building a N-Scale model railroad diorama. It's creative and intuitive, and it flows. AOS is reality slapping you in the face, and entropy kicking you in the gonads. You start out with $10, but you will lose $3 a turn for overhead (2x shares outstanding, plus a '1' speed locomotive), so if you do nothing, you will be bankrupt by Turn 4. There is a sense of urgency, so you'd better get busy livin' or get busy dyin'. Even if you break out of the lower 10 income spaces, you get hit with income reduction (think taxes and depreciation).

The board is tight, so tight that you'll resort to bizarre track layouts to meet your objectives. You have to foresee the future and accurately anticipate how much money you'll need for bidding and track building, and you'll need to anticipate your overhead costs at the end of the turn. The turn order reads like a turn in Squad Leader. There are 10 phases, and everybody does their thing in player turn order, which is determined through bidding, much like RRT, but unlike RRT, if you stick your nose out and bid, you'll pay at least half if you drop out later in the bidding. The one interesting aspect, however, is the selection of actions, like urbanization, going first in deliveries, going first in track building, or upping your locomotive speed (or "links" as they say in AOS), or seeding the production schedule. These actions are selected, like any worker-placement game, on a first-come, first-served basis. Some allow you to "bud in line" and go first, regardless of your position in the turn order. Some are important early in the game, and some are important late the game. Bidding can be furious, as these spaces are crucial to winning, er, surviving the game.

The flow is different between the two games by nature of their turn phases. RRT is like an open framework or a la carte: it essentially allows you to take any three actions per turn, where you select one of six actions. You then collect your income and pay your expenses (dividends). AOS is sequential, like pre fix, and constrained: Three track hex build; two deliveries. Urbanization and upping the locomotive are divorced from this, as they are selected prior to this. New goods cubes may show up randomly on various cities, afterwards instead of the "city growth" card in RRT, which is an action.

RRT flows better in my mind, since the actions of the various players can intertwine, much like the meshing of both strategic and tactical impulses in TI3. RRT play rotates clockwise, which is straight-forward, but is somewhat not as satisfying. AOS play bounces around, much like TI3 per the strategy cards, or like the play order in Power Grid. But regarding the actions themselves, AOS plays like TI2. Everybody does X in this phase, then everybody does Y in next phase, etc. Tedious. 

I noted that Power Grid is a brain-burner, as is AOS or 18xx. But I don't like AOS. It's too analytical and too brutal. At least 18xx, I feel like I'm running a railroad, through the buying of stocks and manipulating the market. The RRT experience is much more vidid than AOS, like I said before--it's like a diorama. Presently, I hate it as much as I hated PR the first time I played it--it just doesn't make sense to me. It has no joy, or at least, I didn't derive any joy out of my first play. I'm willing to try it several more times, as some of my gaming group (the ones that did well) liked it, but I sense this will go the way of Agricola or worse. Get the kerosene ready. I rate it a "7".

RATING: 9
Age of Steam is at heart a game of resource management and tile laying. This solid underlying game is enhanced by mechanics such as the auction for turn order, selection of actions, and goods growth. As with any Martin Wallace design there is some fiddly complexity which adds relatively little to game play (here the pricing system for track-laying is more complicated than it needs to be). The game is punishing to players who fall behind and can sometimes be overlong. But, overall, this is a one of the very best games of its type.

initial rating, don't expect much movement until other train games are played....

Brutal, competitive, unforgiving, punishing, brainburning, enduring, agonizing AND......

GRUELLING, out of all the words this is the best one to describe this game IMO, a real tight economic battle of the minds that will leave you frazzled if you played to the best of your ability, maybe too frazzled and too brainburning reducing the fun somewhat, don't even think of playing this highly strategic game if you haven't eaten your wheaties. I'm not sure yet but it looks to me like the leader is too hard to catch which others complain about as well, then the person losing has a long wait until the game ends which is no good and this certainly detracts. Whoever planned the best at the beginning has the best chance to win but its too bad that by midgame if your 'set-up' is not already up to snuff then it will be too late for you unlike some other games where catching up is possible.

I liked the punishing economic track, this and everything else was very balanced but you are forced to take out shares just to keep afloat, what I couldn't figure out was why you couldn't buy them back later? No biggy there though and it sure felt good to finally get into the "black" after a few turns at least. Playing with more than 4 must be ridiculous by midgame on the regular map, I would try 5 players maybe once just to see but 3 was good and 4 would be much better and probably the sweet spot for me. 

For reasons stated above, I'm not sure how often I would want to play or even get to play even though its a very good game that I do want to play again, otherwise rating could of been a little higher. I hope further playings will cause me to up the rating

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Let me start by saying that Martin Wallace has established himself as my favorite game designer. And it all started for me with Age of Steam. That's not to say I'm his biggest fan, he's certainly put out a few stinkers (Way out West springs right to mind) but overall I enjoy his games very very much.

That being said...Age of Steam is far and away the best of the bunch!  It's very enjoyable but VERY tense at the same time. You're under an extremely tight budget which makes it tough to get going and to keep your head above water...at least initially, well...not for ALL maps. 

Once you do get going the other players will start crowding your space and "stealing" your goods, making it that much more difficult to stay ahead. You spend a ton of resources and time to prepare for some long deliveries and someone jumps in and ruins your plan! Of course, you can, and will, do the same thing to them.

With the number of expansion maps catching up to (surpassing?) the number Formula De maps, the versatility seems endless! If you include the player designed maps available here on the geek, you could play this every week on a different map and STILL not have playing them all in a whole year! 

It's no longer just a simple board game, this is now a game system. If you like train games and you enjoy deep strategy than this is a great choice. 

I prefer this over Railroad Tycoon without an hesitation. In fact, I avoid playing RT to some extent because I want to play it as I would AoS and that just doesn't work. I know a few people that play RT all the time but only because it's softer and easier than AoS.

I haven't played any of the predecessors to AoS and frankly I plan to keep it that way. This is the pinnacle of gaming. Nuf' said.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
"Let's get the basics down: First, this game doesn't like you. It doesn't like anybody else either, but it particularly doesn't like you. If you ever have a question on the rules or how the rules apply to a particular situation then pick the answer that hurts the most. You'll almost certainly be right."  

-- clearclaw



This game is absolutely brutal in its punishment of mistakes.  Small mistakes early can knock you out or put you in a death spiral of debt from which there is no escape.  I know that sounds horrible, but some of my most memorable games are the ones where I managed to hang on until the end without going bankrupt.  I rode the death spiral and survived -- dead last place, but alive.  The game itself is that much fun for me that even knowing that I've carelessly knocked myself out of contention I still have an incentive to see it to the end.

The mechanism that provides the largest chance for death-spiral-type mistakes is taking shares (i.e. loans).

A share provides you with a one-time payment (varies based on the map) in exchange for interest being due every turn, including the turn that the share was taken out.  Taking out a share also permanently reduces your end-of-game score.

Everyone starts the game with two shares, which means that interest is due at the end of turn one.  The clock has started, and everyone is behind the curve from the beginning.

How many shares to take out is a difficult decision, because you have to do this before you do anything else that turn.  Therefore, you'll have one chance to take out the right amount of debt before you have to perform the following:

* Auction for turn order   roles.
* Build track.
* Collect revenue.
* Pay interest on debt   locomotive upkeep.

The last three can be mostly calculated out ahead of time, although opponents could block your track routes or steal your deliveries.  When you add in the auctions as well, it becomes a lot cloudier.  You have to be able to make educated guesses about what everyone else wants to do, and how much you think they're willing to pay for it.  You need to balance that with your own needs and how much you're willing to pay.

It sounds complex, because it is.  But it's so agonizingly awesome.

RATING: 9
Rating based on a few games only. The game is fun, exciting, has plenty of player interaction, an elegant design, and it *feels* like you're running railroads. On the bad side, it has quite a bit of downtime while players ponder their moves, and it feels like players can get screwed sometimes, through no fault of their own.

Also, the physical quality of the game leaves a bit to be desired. The game could use a bit of "German" shining up; nicer board, a bit of graphics on the charts, etc., just to give the game that bit more "chrome". Doing the "Attila" thing and using the same colors for players and goods was also a bad choice.

All in all, a nice game. I think I prefer it with fewer (3-4) players, rather than with 5-6.

RATING: 9
Brutal, in every sense of the word. I love the tight economy, how you have to eke out every ounce of profit, and can't waste anything if you intend on winning.

What I don't like is how random the cubes are.  Relying on a few dice rolls is never something I like - if you roll them a lot, it's not such a big deal, as everything averages out, but when they're only rolled a few times, and it can drastically affect the game?  I'll pass.  Steam handles this aspect of the game better, though not tremendously so.

I also don't particularly like that the game is so single-minded. You build track, and move cubes around, take loans (or, er, issue shares), and, well, do it all over again!  It's nice and simple, easy to pick up, but it almost feels to me like Age of Steam is a part of what should be a more complex, involved, complete game system.  Maybe I need to play an 18xx game.  I'd like to.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Not for everyone.  Interactive AF.
