=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I doubt that I will ever play Age of Steam with 5 or 6 players ever again... you seem to lose control of what you are doing.  One mistake and you are completely shut out from scoring big for the rest of the game... and you just sit there like a schmuck for another two hours, twiddling your thumbs while the other players gain from your misfortune.  And regardless of what others say... it always happens, even with people who have all played the game before.  Fun?... I don't think so.

Even when playing with fewer players, if someone takes an early lead, it's VERY hard to catch him, especially if he's lucky enough to get a few quick random goods placed on his connected cities.   

The penalties in the game far outweigh any benefits.  Starting capital is way too low and upkeep is too high.  You cannot win the game by trying to keep shares to a minimum (a serious design flaw), and the 'income reduction' rule, which penalizes players for doing too well, is just plain silly (the only reason I can see why this rule exists is because of the stated 'runaway leader' problem... it's an attempt to fix).  Too many house rules are required to make this one better.

A lot of people here on BGG applauded 2nd edition Funkenschlag (Power Grid) for trimming down the chrome and extras in a fairly complex game system and distill the original game down to its basics.   However, Age of Steam could be well be considered as a redevelopment of the Early Railways system (e.g-Lancashire Rails, Volldampf, etc.)... which had much cleaner, simpler, and more elegant designs.  There seems to be a double standard here for BGGers who like both games... give me Volldampf over this bloated edition any day of the week.

I'll still play this game with a little coaxing, but if other games are available, I'll probably pass.

I should add that now that I've played Railroad Tycoon, if both RRT and AoS are being offered, it's RRT all the way.

Update:  I played AoS again after a long hiatus.  Even though we played a rule slightly wrong (which didn't affect the outcome), my original evaluation of this game has not changed... it is still much too dry and demanding... not fun at all.

RATING: 10
AoS is not a 'nice' game... though once this is fully understood it slowly transcends itself into a very satisfying experience.

3rd edition, (Rust Belt) US Great Lakes area map (in my neck of the woods, twixt Michigan City and Ft Wayne).  I first owned/played Steam and later picked up AoS, just to find out what all the fuss was about.  I currently tend to favor Steam over AoS as I'm not a big fan of auctions in general and the Goods Growth/Production mechanics seems over the top, both of which are rather time consuming IMO.  I've noticed in subsequent expansion maps that it's the Production system that is changed in an attempt to simplify or improve upon seeding the board with Goods.  My rating will most likely increase as I'm able to get my family members to spend a little more time to appreciate and embrace the dollar auction method of Turn Order which I'm truly intrigued by and feel is especially important in a multi-action network building type game like this.     

I'm not crazy about the general layout and lack in effort with vague examples and illustrations in this '3rd' edition rulebook.  A full area map in the beginning of the rules illustrating setup would have been most helpful allowing for further referencing throughout.  For example: GG/P, page 12, proper illustrations could have easily cleared up any misconception regarding the current convoluted instructions to the light/dark in relation to starting/new cities in the Goods Growth phase of the rulebook.  There is no excuse for a game in it's 3rd edition not to have addressed pre-existing loose ends or FAQs.  No intro and/or ultimate goal?  ...seems rushed.

UPDATE: I'm getting to enjoy the harshness of this game, even more so than Steam.  Something about the challenge... just to stay afloat, win or lose.  I read an interview (by Matt Thrower) with Martin Wallace and he mentions, "I don't like player elimination, I would like to think that some of my games are still enjoyable to play even if you know you cannot win."  I like that.

RATING: 5
Tricky mental exercise here. With a limited number of turns you really feel pressured to choose the right role each time. You want to build track and move goods, but pay as little as possible to do so. Issuing shares can give you a cash boost, but at a continuing cost in dividends paid out.

With this game in particular the vagaries of the dice rolls led to a paucity of goods in cities that linked up well with players' networks. The mid-game devolved into a simple bidding war for the Locomotive action, won of course by the player with the most cash on hand at the time. Monopolizing this action let him block out the others who had longer potential links stymied. 

There is probably a great reason for it, but I wasn't digging the point reduction at the end of each turn, since it acted like a catch-up feature in a video game.

RATING: 10
AoS is not complex rules-wise, but it does force difficult decisions in almost every phase of the game. The game challenges you to think in both spacial and economic terms, devising profitable (often circuitous) routes while keeping expenses manageable. As a system, the game is remarkably versatile - as is evidenced by the many expansions, most with their own rules variations. The rating of 10 is for the system, even though the game as published would only have limited replayability with just the base map. If you enjoy this and think you will play it a lot, there are a lot of great expansions out there, both officially and unofficially produced.

Yes, the scoring and the income reduction reek of "gaminess". The production action is too weak with the use of dice to determine which cities produce. I used to rate the game lower for these reasons, but now that I've enjoyed a few expansions, and I've seen how flexible the rules are to allow all sorts of different game play, I can only throw up my hands in admiration and say that this is one of the best games ever conceived.

I do prefer the harsh economics of AoS to the softened up version, Steam. Even still, Steam is quite excellent and if Age of Steam sounds too punishing for your tastes, I highly recommend giving Steam a try.

RATING: 7

Base Map:
I've now played at all player counts (3-6) on the base map; my preference for this map is 5>4>>>6>>>>>>3. 5 player sessions are the tightest, 4 player sessions maintain tightness but with more more elbow room, 6 player sessions are too long and too crowded and 3 player sessions are too loose and open. I'd be hard pressed to play it with 3 again as the overall experience feels quite watered down.

I know there are different maps for different player counts; however, most maps change fundamental rules which means having to re-learn the rules each time out and that sucks. My group isn't dedicated enough to make owning multiple maps worth it. I still really like AoS but until I get a map that scales well from 3-4 players AoS will spend more time on the shelf than at the table.

RATING: 7
An easy 8 after one play. Rating could go up. 

A turn of [GameID=4098] is quite simple. You begin by deciding how many shares of stock to issue. You can't go too overboard because you can only issue a maximum of 15 shares over the course of 7 turns. You owe 20% interest on each share during each turn of the game, so you need to balance operating capital against potentially overwhelming debt. The money is only used to bid on drafting order for a set of once-per-turn advantages (like making the first shipment or upgrading your shipping capacity) and laying new track. So you need to be able to afford what you want, when you want it. Beyond that you need to position your track on the board so that you can ship the most lucrative goods before other players, make it difficult for other players to connect to key cities, and make it likely that other players will occasionally use your own shipping lines. That's Age of Steam. Simple, challenging, [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thing/4098-Age%20of%20Steam#linkeditems]expandable[/url], and probably addictive. For what it's worth, I think this game is night-and-day better than [GAMEID=17133]. 

Several aspects of its reputation are overblown. Some find the game too punishing, and harp on the possibility of player elimination. Maybe if you were introducing the game to a casual player and didn't give a simple explanation of the game, you [b]might[/b] see a player eliminate themselves. Otherwise you won't. Moreover, I don't find the game punishing, because for the first two-thirds of the game, your goal is more or less to break even as you lay more track and upgrade your shipping capability. Profitability comes later. 

Update: the coming of Merchant of Venus has really made me question whether this is an 8 for me. I need to give this a couple of more plays to distinguish it from the RTT family. I'd also like to try the new Railways of England map and I suppose Steam as well. But this whole Wallace train game clusterf*** is growing tiresome. Perhaps just not my genre.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Age of Steam is a game of meticulous planning: You need to size up the entire game at the beginning, assess the board and decide what you will try to do both short-term and long-term. At the beginning of each turn you need to estimate how the entire turn will play out right down to a few dollars, as one share too many is the frying pan and one share too few is the fire. Throughout the game you need to be well aware of the entire board state and tweak your game plan according to opportunities opening and closing.
 
Sounds like a lot to take in? I didn't mention yet that you're not just doing this for yourself. You have to have a good idea of what all your opponents are up to as well.
 
If that's still not enough, each expansion map introduces a few twists to the basic rules. I finished a distant last in my second game of Age of Steam not only because my tactical mind is not well suited to the general task of the game, but also because the little plan I did have ran against the rule tweaks for the map in question.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
If I had to pick one single game as my favorite, Age of Steam would be it. 

The combination of having to decide in advance how many shares to issue (to get working capital), followed by a brutal turn order / special power auction is delicious. And while some people seem to think that the board play is near-trivial, I think there are some really interesting and hard decisions on short term vs. long term optimization in track building. 

And that feeling of claustrophobia and desperation when turns come and go, but you're still not profitable and are starting to run out of expansion options. Many of my favourite gaming memories involve games of Age of Steam gone horribly bad, and trying to claw myself into profitability by the end of the game. Not to win, but just to not suffer the humiliation of not even being able to break even.

One thing to beware is that AoS does require careful map selection for the optimum effect. With enough new players, you can play on the rust belt map and have fun. With experts, you really need something a lot tighter to get that "knife fight in a phonebooth" feeling that few other games can provide.

Luckily I have friends who are obsessive enough to own just about all published AoS expansions without actually owning the game. So it's a symbiotic relationship: I provide the base game, they dig up the perfect map for the occasion.

RATING: 10
Based on 10+ plays (35+ if you include expansions).  Yes, it's brutal to beginners.  Yes, I play with a pen and paper to keep up with the math.  And yes, it's not exactly the Mona Lisa of board game graphic design.  But despite these foibles, AoS is still currently my favorite game.  Unlike a number of overly tactical Euro games, it demands that you plan many turns ahead.  And unlike the excessively strategic Euro games, it inserts enough chaos (via auctions, goods growth, and other players) to force you to adjust those plans every turn.  My only real criticism of the game is that occasionally it forces a player, who may have no real chance of winning the game, into an explicit king-making role.

I also own:
AoS Expansion #1 - England & Ireland
AoS Expansion #2 - Western US & Germany
AoS Expansion #3 - Scandinavia & Korea
AoS Expansion - 1830's Pennsylvania & Northern California

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Excellent strategic economic railway game with track laying and pick-up & deliver mechanisms. Generating a selfsustaining economy is crucial, so don't be afraid to take shares (but don't overdo it: you might go bancrupt or regret it during the victory point count if you end in second place by one point). Get as fast as possible to the point that you earn more money than you spend. Bump up the links for your locomotive as fast as possible, because more links will result in higher income. Keep on building track, as this not only results in points at the end of the game but also provides alternative options during the game. The initial random goods placement ensures variability in game play. Look also for the expansions!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This rules of this excellent game are distilled down to pretty much a pure Euro essence.  A simple mechanic but every action in every phase of every move is significant.  The intensity makes AoS not appropriate for every gaming night or group but an excellent game for me.

RATING: 9
PLUS
Kombinacja różnych aspektów (finansowy, budowniczy, licytacyjny, atrybutywny poprzez wybór akcji i stochastyczny poprzez dostawy nowych towarów); każda decyzja krytyczna; przy pomocy ciekawych algorytmów zawiera grę w grze (mechanika licytacyjna).
MINUS
Możliwy lekki problem koronacji pod koniec gry.
SUMA
Może jeszcze nie perfekcyjna gra gospodarczo-kolejowa, ale najlepsza.

PLUS
Combination of various aspects (financial, engineering, bidding, attributive by action selection and stochastic by emerging of new goods); every decision is critical; contains a game in game by means of interesting algorithms (bidding mechanism).
MINUS
Possible kingmaker problematic in the endgame.
SUM
Maybe not a perfect economic train game, but the best one.

RATING: 9
To quote Dorothy Parker's review of the The Last Post; 'It is a novel to be read with a furrow in the brow . . . Yet The Last Post is a novel worth all its difficulties.'  Age of Steam matches this experience.

Beautiful pain.  AoS has beaten me up so bad, I feel on some moves I have been in a collision course with a steam engine, but I will always come back for more. If you come out the other end intact with your wits about you then you have earnt it.  This is a game which gives no one an easy ride.

Avoiding further puns I feel this game is closest to a classic modern novel from a powerhouse like DeLilo or Pynchon.  Maybe difficult to grasp at first but captivating and ultimately rewarding.  A game for those boardgame literate, not a starter game, who wish to challenge themselves as much as their friends. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
What a delicious struggle this game is!  You struggle to start turning a profit (and not issue too many shares), to do well enough in the auction to get the action that you *really* want, to get routes before others do, to ship goods before others do, to find the right times to upgrade your locomotive (having to pass up on some other juicy action or income in the process), and to find & create clever routes that will earn you the biggest income.  A Top 10 game for me.

RATING: 6
This game has been replaced by Steam.  If you were considering whether to get this game or Steam, I would get Steam.  The mechanic is pickup and deliver and plays almost the same as Steam, but city growth is too random and Steam does city growth much better.  

Age of Steam requires that bid for turn order and it is difficult to value the bid if you are new player.  It does have dozens of maps.  If you want to invest in many maps, you may want to get Age of Steam.  

I have sseen that players who like Steam tend to find the AoS rules annoying.  AoS players don't like Steam as there are simplifications that AoS players find annoying.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
This is my favourite 'pick-up and deliver' game in a time frame of 2-3 hours
+ 
little luck
Enormous variability with dozens of expansions ; with one ruleset you can play more than 30 different games.
Extreme tension begins already in the first round ; I have always that feeling of doom hanging around. Will you have a cube for next delivery or not ?  Is somebody else going to take your goods ? high screwage factor.
In every round you have to take tactical and strategical decisions ; you have to plan many rounds in advance. 
Money management is crucial ; every dollar counts.
- 
1)Not suitable for every group ; very harsh and brutal game ; this game creates sometimes a negative atmosphere in the group when somebody is blocked or when somebody picks up that crucial cube just before you...
2)In many games there is a sort of anticlimax curve; the first 3 rounds of the game are  often crucial and very tense ; you can then already predict who is still in the race ; so in many games there's a runaway leader problem. The last 2 rounds are in many cases a parade round for the future winner. If you are coming from behind, you know you can win... next time !
3)Luck factor plays a certain role at the start of the game ; if others players start in the same or neigbouring city, you can be blocked out for the rest of the game and you can forget your winning ambitions !
4)There is sometimes  a problem of kingmaking because you can lend a route from other players.A not skilled player can give points to one player because of bad play and so give an unintentional  advantage  to some one else. 

Ideal number of players for 'standard' expansions : plays best with 4. 
with 3 : too much open space. 
with 5-6 players : game is too aggressive and chaotic ;only tactical play is possible.  

Some expansions reduce significantly the mentioned disadvantages of the game.

Favourite expansions:
- AoS Mars : 
The track laying system is very complex and excruciating. 
You cannot be blocked in the first round ; the preprogrammed start position is a big bonus for me ; fixes the mentioned problem.
- AoS New Hampshire : 
Very brutal route building system
Kingmaking is impossible in this expansion because you may not borrow a track from another player. In this expansion there's a heavy struggle for acces to key cities.
- AoS New England : 'crossing interstate lines' and 'smuggling action'  is a very clever mechanic ; this system is first used in AoS Moon but I still never played this expansion.

So why do I love this game ? Yes, there's some kingmaking, yes there is sometimes a runaway leader problem, and a(too) high screwage factor... but this is the most exciting game I know, full of tension and "turn angst".  Strategic decisions are in many cases more important than tactical interests.  In fact it's a sort of a "train wargame" 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
I've said in my review of Wikinger that Eurogames have themes because nobody would want to play an abstract game of that complexity. Age of Steam is, to me, a complex abstract, and unsurprisingly I don't want to play it (again).

I can see that it provides a rather unique challenge and how experience might help to get quicker at the tremendously fiddly endgame, thus improving the density of actual decisions. However, there are just too many excellent and far more accessible games in my cupboard to make me delve deeper into this.

Played once.

RATING: 9
Oh, the tension, the drama, the agony!

This game makes you feel like you're losing almost the entire time, and I love it.  Maybe it's the masochist in me, but I find the money tension in this game to be very enjoyable.  It's not for everyone, though.

After many plays I have moved my rating from an 8 to a 9 due to the number of expansions and the variety they bring to Age of Steam.  

Mean, but productive, play in this game is often passive aggressive.  The tactics are subtle.  Overt attacks will often just hurt you more in the end. 

Based on 10+ plays from 2 to 6 players on various maps.

RATING: 6
I like this game, however I need to be in the right mood for it. The game can be a little dry and I have never been thrilled with the production. I am not going ot get into the AoS vs RRT thing, but I think they both have their pluses and minuses. There are a few things however thatI am not sure of in this game. 
1) its unforgiving. if you make a mistake on turn one it can be over. You may not go bankrupt but you have little or no chance of winning. 
2) it is possible for someone to king make easily in this game by running block they do not need on someone elses track either to deny someone winning by using a block they need or to prop up aother person's score.
3) some of the roles seem next to worthless for most of the game.
4) some of the scoring seems over complicated for no reason. The income reduction, the track bits at the end. None of it feels like it belongs and people lose games due to not knowing about it or not having mastered how to max points out of it. That feels a bit gamey to me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Game in a Nutshell:  Borrow money, lay track, and deliver goods to earn income and score the most points.

Played as:  3,4 player

Pros:  Has a great blend of planning with some player and game unpredictability thrown in to make it interesting. The game is a stream of constant important tactical decisions where adapting as the game progresses is an absolute must.  I love the constant interaction through the auction and into the building/moving goods phase when name-calling is almost obligatory.  The best part is the satisfaction associated with taking your little train / handful of money, and grinding your way to building a large rails network, with a vastly upgraded train, that’s competing in a spaghetti bowl of other player’s tracks to hopefully turn the greatest delivery points. 

Cons:  Poor presentation values. The ugliest of all "Steam" games with poop-y artwork and the third edition plastic trains only seem to clash with the whole package. The gray-scale mats also look cheap.  Needing a formula to tally the final score is kind of a drag. It’s not as natural of a flow to the game as I would have expected. A well presented but simple player aid would seem like a no brainer however is not included. 

Other:  It appears to me that way too much is made of the threat of going bankrupt early in the game.  Any game explainer worth their salt should be able to easily avoid this with a little warning and minimal hand-holding. I thought the game was a little too wide open with three players and much preferred it with four as bumping heads became almost immediate. What keeps the game from scoring higher marks for me is that gameplay comes across as more of an ever-changing puzzle than a game that allows for creative tactics.

Best Use:  When you need the most solid of train games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This has to be the most excrutiatingly tightfisted game I've ever played ... and this is what makes it so great.  Wallace's design is flawless and I'm astonished at how he worked out the math to make it such a perfectly tough money management game.  You basically start off in the red.  You need to issue shares to get money but you have to pay back 20% of each share issued for the rest of the game.  You need money to bid for turn order, etc and to build tracks.  Overall, you need a great strategy and probably need to use your opponent's tracks (which pays them) to deliver goods in order to make the money you need to hopefully get out of the red before the mid-game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Not as enthusiastic as I once was about this one, and after a multitude of playings, am coming to the conclusion that the game is flawed. Things I like: Its a brain burner. Tight resources make for an intense gaming session, requiring constant thought and planning. Players teeter on the brink of bankruptcy early on, and its a challenge trying to avoid it. Things I dont like:1. There can be a runaway winner problem in some games; players left alone in their own regions can sometimes become very powerfull. 2. Bad starting board position can ruin you for the whole game. 3. Rules need lots of clarification in some areas, as well as better examples. 4. Bidding doesnt seem so significant anymore as players tend to just drop out and still get what they want. 5. A lot of complex track tiles never seems to get used. 6. Whaddya mean you cant buy and sell shares?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Based on comments here, I thought Age of Steam would be a more "strategic," "tighter," and more "tense" game than Steam. After trying it out, my reactions are "barely," "not really," and "Who cares?"

Yes, the Production display shows where goods will be going, but we're talking about a difference between knowing the location of three (Steam) and five (Age of Steam) goods per city. If this is such a big deal, why not just place five goods in every city to start? The answer is "tension" and "uncertainty," but I call this "luck": some cities will get their goods early, while others will get them late, based on random rolls. Sure you can "calculate probabilities," but that doesn't make me any more interested in guessing whether a coin's going to come up heads or tails. If allowing players to place goods via Production and Urbanization preserves the "tension" and tosses the luck at the expense of a little strategy, so be it.

As for being "tighter," this seems almost entirely based on the share limit. While one player nearly went bankrupt in my one game of Age of Steam, as a whole it didn't feel that much more stringent than Steam (Standard rules). If this is really important to some players, then just apply a limit of fifteen shares in Steam. (Age of Steam die-hards readily dismiss Steam's Basic Game by using similar tweaks.)

Finally, there's more "tension" in the auction, which I suppose I can actually understand. There just aren't as many good actions in Age of Steam, so third and frequently second place in the auction get hosed. Were I more interested in turn order auctions, I might appreciate this, but generally I find them pretty boring, especially relative to the additional time they require. In this case, I actually like Base Steam's action selection and am curious as to how this would work with the Standard Game's financial rules.

Ultimately, as others have pointed out, there's really no need to own both. Personally, I recommend getting Steam and then printing out the Production and Income charts for Age of Steam if you ever want to use the latter's rules.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Played one game.  I've a big fan of Steam.  And I liked Railroad Tycoon quite a bit.  I think I may like this one the best.  Yes, it is brutal.  I think I may like to have Steam just for a little friendlier game for some.  But when you want the real thing, this is it.  I can see why it gets such high praise.

Update: I've played several games on the Rustbelt map as well as half a dozen other maps and AoS has become maybe my favorite game.  I've bought about 50 more maps and started a monthly game, trying to make my friends into converts as well.  Just love it.  

Some favorite maps so far: Northern California, Scandinavia, Europe.  I have a dozen more I really want to try but I keep getting newbies so I'm hoping soon we'll get a regular group that will be up for the challenges of new maps.

As an aside, I take a little issue with the cost of some of these maps.  I love the Bezier designs by Ted Alspach's because I consider they are reasonably priced.  I hate the unmounted boards of some other designers and I don't care how rare some of these are. Spending $60 or more for thin paper maps is just ridiculous and I don't understand why these people print such limited quantities.  I've bought a few, e.g. Madagascar/Holland, which I look forward to trying but I just can't stomach paying so much for them again.  They just look cheap.

RATING: 4
Rating of 4 based on one 5-player session. I don't understand all the hype over this game. It just doesn't feel like a train-game at all. There's WAY too much downtime while players minmax their choices. 6-players is far too many for this game - I think its really a 3-4 player game. The game system has a non-sensical goods delivery system, an over-random economic model and counter-intuitive scoring system. The income reduction rule seems like a kludged check on breakaway leaders, but its ineffective. It reminds me of an awkward implementation of the Power Grid system. The production values seems to be at odds with its high retail price, too, but I guess you're buying all the bits for use in the expansions. Its shorter than most 18XX games, but I'd rather spending 6 hours on 1830, for example, and come last than play AoS and win. I can't see myself leaping at the next opporunity to play AoS. However, I'd be willing to play this game again to change my opinion, but only with a clear explanation/reading of the rules beforehand.

RATING: 7
Another train game from Martin Wallace that is similar to Volldampf. Issue shares for money, build track and deliver goods. This could be Wallace's best game. It has a very steep learning curve, and best played with experienced players. Difficult decisions on how many shares to issue, how high to bid in the player turn order, and which tracks to build. The tight income throughout the game makes it a very tense game. It's a good game, but it does clock in at least 3 hours.

Rating bumped up based on two things:  1) Playing with 6 players is far better than 4 or 5 IMO.  2) After a few plays, seeing the networks emerge ahead of time is easier to do, which decreases the amount of braindrain experienced.  Not a lot, but enough to improve the work:fun ratio.

--update---
Game continues to make mincemeat out of me, but at least I have some desire to improve rather than simply burn it.

--update--
The steady rise in my ratings of AoS is a testament to the veracity of statements regarding the game's learning curve.  Taking the time to get over the hump is definitely rewarded.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
-

This is a very good game, but not great, despite its super high regard on this board. I find it somewhat fiddly and the luck factor is too high for such a long strategy game. It gets too slow with more than 3 players and the luck and randomness go up too. Furthermore, to mitigate the runaway leader problem, which the game is highly susceptible to, an 'income reduction' mechanism has been devised. I find this tool inelegant and even crude. I wish this was handled differently. Still, the game is very engaging, has high replayability value, and creates a tense and competitive mood.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Ouch. Just, ouch. This game simply despises you, despises your family, your hopes, your dreams, your cat, all of it. As a game system it is really, really mean. Everyone bids for different abilities with money they can't afford to lose, then they all build unaffordable personal track on a shared board to try and move precious cubes between cities for desperately needed income. Money is tight, your plans can be utterly ruined by another player's bid being a dollar higher than you were anticipating, and someone can easily steal a delivery you badly needed to avoid spiraling into debt. It punishes mistakes, gives you choices between horrible things (so you choose the least bad), and it requires money to move forward yet is incredibly stingy with it. 

One of my favorite things about this game is how money is scarce until the very end, when the surviving players finally become profitable…just in time for cash to immediately become valueless (the rules state "money is just a tool"). Sometimes I lose this game and lose badly, finishing in 5th place out of 5 players, but there is still a sense of accomplishment at having made it to the finish line. 

By last count I own 25 Expansion maps, which is patently absurd given how infrequently I've played this and given how grim my prospects are for getting others interested in playing this. But that merely demonstrates how compelling I find this, that I am willing to give it a leash of almost unmeasurable length purely on the  off chance that some day someone else falls for this exercise in self-flagellation the same way I have. I will keep waiting, for some day my prince will come.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I'm somewhat ambivalent about Age of Steam.  Some games it seems like the best game ever with players vying for resources, racing to build track and competitive bidding for turn order.  Other times it feels like a tedious math exercise.

Players bid for turn order at the beginning of each turn, then they select special actions, build track and ship cubes.  The farther you ship cubes the more you income increases.  Finances, especially in the beginning, can be very tight.  It's even possible to go bankrupt and get eliminated from the game.  I find this to be an excellent and invigorating part of the game.

The end game feels less tense to me.  Likely because I haven't figured out what I'm doing yet.  But also because there comes a time in the game where you can lay the spectre of bankruptcy to rest.

I used to have the Warfrog version, but traded it.  Recently picked up the Eagle Games version.  I thought the plastic bits would annoy me, but the production values are amazing and I'm forced to admit I like it better.  Though I think the box isn't as thick which means less room for expansions.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
September 2015: Downgrade from 4 to 2. Really do not want to play this again.

March 2011: Downgrade from 6 to 4. Simply not fun. 

Winning strategy: Deliver the most 6-link goods!

----------------

My dad prefers playing with half the board rather than playing with two colors. (The game is less fiddly and plays much faster.) I think my dad likes this game the same reason he likes Puerto Rico: intuitive rules, turns go by quickly, good theme and game mechanics integration.

I'm worried about replayability, though: Because we play this two player (no other willing opponents in the house), and the locations of the cities are fixed, the tile-laying aspect might get repetitive. A more significant problem (but this might be because I’ve only played this two player) is that some of the actions are totally useless, making many of the auctions for turn order bland affairs.

UPDATE: Downgraded from an 8 to a 6. This is *too much* of a gamer's game! You need highly competitive auctions right at the beginning of the game in order for the tension to last throughout the game. If that doesn't happen, then the most experienced player would get an insurmountable lead. That's probably the reason why I think this game feels a bit too long, and why some of the auctions feel useless near the end of the game, that insurmountable lead problem.

I actually prefer this game the most as two-player, with the half-board variant.

Just play 1830 instead!

RATING: 9.5
The upside:
-It's the closest you'll ever get to a Cinderella's mother-in-law kind of experience. It's mean, demanding, unforgiving, makes you cry, and doesn't care to recognize how pretty you are on the inside. 
-How is the last point a plus? Well, when It ends, no matter what, you feel rewarded, you survived (hopefuly), and that must be like getting a prince...like Cinderella.

The downside:
-Wouldn't this be improved, at least 1%, by taking the production to more deterministic levels?

RATING: 9.5
To be honest I`ve never been particularly into railway games, but this one is a true gamer`s gem. AoS is tense, gripping and hard. It constantly pushes you to think thoroughly about your and opponents` next moves. If you want to win that is.. Not just tactics but subtle strategy with a strong theme on it too. This game is not for casual players though and playing time is two hours minimum, 3 hours on average with more players. Learning curve is at first steep, but the game mechanics in itself are quite simple once you get the hang of them. Production qualities of the components are ok but could be better: coins and wooden tokens are very nice and track tiles are sturdy and functional, map is thick but not even close to being beautiful, income track and goods chart are just thick paper with black print on them. So don`t expect a pretty game with fancy graphics & chrome but instead a more spartan look. Anyways gamewise this is getting better play by play and that`s what matters in the end. IMHO even better than Puerto Rico. Highly recommended.

RATING: 9
Brutal.

A struggle to remain solvent. Players begin saddled with debt. As players take loans to pay for track builds and to bid for turn order, that debt inevitably increases.

Worse, a player can become stuck in a vicious cycle. He or she might take more debt to pay for the debt they've already accrued.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Likes
A satisfying pick up and deliver financial game that creates brutal player interaction and rewards good planning. The train theme is well integrated and fun to play. The auction mechanic creates fantastic agony in deciding whether to stay in or drop out.

Dislikes
I really dislike the way cubes enter play. The randomness in this one area is so glaring in light of the pure choice driven gameplay of the rest of the design. The special action of placing random cubes on the city growth chart so that they might come out is such a ridiculously underpowered choice compared to the other actions that it bothers me to no end that it is there.

First play: 2008

RATING: 8
Difficult pick up and deliver game that has you on the verge of bankruptcy through the first part of the game and it really feels like an accomplishment when you finally break through and make a profit. I've been playing some of the expansion maps on this lately, and find myself missing some of the refinements introduced by the newer Steam. I don't like the way goods come on to the board in this game, and, while I appreciate the tension involved in issuing stock and deciding how much money you need for the turn, I think that this game is much more prone to AP and longer play times because of it. I do enjoy the auctions, but, again, the enjoyment of them comes at the cost of a longer play time. Longer, more brutal, less refined, more brain-burning and with more downtime, I give the edge to Steam as far as implementations of the system. I DO like this game though, I would just prefer to play Steam if given a choice.

I find that I am thinking about what I might want to do differently in the games played, which is usually a good sign that the game has some traction of appeal for me.

RATING: 4
Simply not as good as the original (Lancashire/New England Railways). This game could be seen from orbit to be a Martin Wallace game: you want to love it, you can tell it's almost fantastically good, but you realize it's just another flawed game.

2010: I've played on a variety of maps now and still like it. I wish the production weren't random though; that's my main gripe with the game.

RATING: 8
Age of Steam myths:

It is complicated:  sure, it has some rules depth, but i wouldnt definately not call this a complex game.  No moreso than many medium-heavy euros.

It is brutal.  This isnt so much of a myth, but it is overstated sometimes.  Unforgiving would be a more accurate word.  Theoretically player elimination can happen, though anyone who understands the rules(green or experienced) can easily avoid it.  

Long term planning is why i love this game.  It seems that so many games are more tactical in depth anymore and dont reward a sound long term strategy. 

This is a great game for people who like to burn their brains on upcoming turns.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Record: 3-3

I hate income reduction. It's ridiculous that your late game big plays that you built up to are worth less because you bumped into the next income reduction bracket. It's ridiculous that you sometimes want to do otherwise sub-optimal things based solely on your position on the income track. And it's also ridiculous that your opponent might deprive himself of 2 points just to push you into the next Income Reduction bracket.

The game uses this income reduction to force players to upgrade their locomotives and keep doing bigger deliveries (because each turn your deliveries are worth less once you hit income reduction)... so you are not only playing against your opponents, but also the game. I don't see how that's necessary, you win if you outscore your opponents - why does the game need to step in and kick everyone back at these discrete intervals?

Railroad Tycoon's version of income reduction is brilliant - as your score goes up, your income peaks and then comes down. In AoS, you [i]VPs[/i] get reduced, not just your income. Income and VPs should be split, as they are in Railroad Tycoon, or in Brass.

Having to take shares of stock up front is also a point of contention for me. All it really does is make the game take longer, as you have to meticulously plan out your turn, and probably a backup plan, because taking 1 share too many is brutal, and being short $1 can be an [i]even bigger[/i] kick in the nuts.

RATING: 6.5
Good but not great game.  Almost total lack of ability to affect the leader and many of the abilities you bid for are very weak in comparison to the best ones.  The game really seems to have only one valid strategy (issue stocks and build lots of track).  Virtually no cooperation between players.  Even with all these failures it is still a good game, especially if you play with a few house rules which make some of the abilities better such as engineering giving one addition *free* track instead of just 1 free track, the first turn ability allowing you to always go first the next round not just allow you to pass once, and production allows you to force a reroll of the goods dice once.

RATING: 7.75

2nd Edition. Apparently still on a sllloooowww boat to the US. Seems like Warfrog doesn't think much of the US market...

The game is very intense. Players need to be aware of every element on the board (city placement, goods placement, etc) and have alternative plans.

RATING: 8
[2011-07-30]
Örülök, hogy adtam még egy esélyt a vonatos játékok királyának. Igaz nem a Steamet próbáltam ki újra, hanem a nagy elődöt, az Age of Steamet, amit még nehezebbnek írnak, mint az új változatot. Ennek ellenére most esett le a játék minden apró és kevésbé apró mechanizmusa. A tavalyi rossz élményeket most a szinte tökéletes játékélmény váltotta fel, szinte minden megvilágosodott a játék alatt, pedig már semmire nem emlékeztem szinte a Steam szabályokból. Így kezdem látni a játék mélységeit és azt miért dicsérik annyira ezt a játékot. Ehhez biztos hozzátett az, hogy azóta sok komoly gémer játék megismerésén vagyok túl. Az előd egyik régi változatával játszottam, aminek kivitele nem éppen mondható szépnek, de valahogy ez is eltörpült most a játékélmény mellett. Különbség az új változathoz képest, hogy itt az árukockák kockadobással kerülnek föl, de egy nagyon jó kis mechanizmussal a városokra, a pénzszerzés is más kicsit. Valahogy nekem itt minden jobban bejött, mint a Steam esetében, bár lehet ha most játszanék azzal újra már sokkal jobban tetszene az is. Összességében aki egy komoly vonatos játékra vágyik, annak bátran ajánlható az Age of Steam, jó kis komoly gémer játék, de azért a nehézségétől nagyon megijedni nem kell.

[2012-07-29]
3-an is működik a játék. Kicsit az árukockák felkerülésének szerencséjét lehet hibáztatni, amit a Steamban kicsit orvosoltak is, de ha jól belegondolunk előre lehet látni milyen városra milyen kocka kerül így minden számolható. Nagyon jó játék az Age of Steam. Emelek is a ponton.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Certainly, one of the most brilliant games I've played.  Perfect for what it does.  This game is like treading water hoping to keep afloat before the rescue squad comes.  The joy in the agony of making crucial decisions is only surpassed by watching your fellow players squirm.  Easily one of the most brutal games created.  For how complex the decisions are, it is amazing how simple the gameplay is.  Perhaps a little long and exhausting for regular play, but this game has now become an event.  I foresee this taking up a long evening once every six months (which in my book doesn't get much better).

UPDATE: I find myself wanting to play this all the time, and have found that I can now get some regular play out of the system.  The more I get games on the table the more I want to play this.  With all the maps out there I just can't get enough...  

UPDATE:  I've come to learn that this is my favorite game.

I rate all the maps on their own scale compared to each other, so a map rated 7 is still a ten in my book as it is more AoS and therefore better than anything else in my book.

[b]PNP:[/b]
JAPAN (7): Like all other games set in Japan, the maps restrict movement and the long banana makes for difficulties when everyone wants to get through.  Normally this is fine, but there are essentially three distinct parts of the map with the center able to handle two players and the two outer regions able to handle one each.  The problem with a four player game is that if one area can't allow for a good starting position with an uneven spread, you end up with two players cutting each other's guts out while the other two build a wonderful network.  Sharing is usually a good thing in AoS, but when the Locomotive action isn't permanent to lose a ship to the upgrade makes for less inclination to share.  Players stuck in the back have a much harder time advancing as they need both ships to stay afloat, whereas lead players can very easily afford to upgrade their locomotive.  I'd play it again, but only with three.

RATING: 8
[Warfrog 2nd (2002) edition] {Railroad business; dice-rolling, action-drafting, auction, variable-player-order, pick-up-and-deliver, player elimination; optimal 4p} AoS is a nice pick-up-and-deliver game. The many expansions add different 'tastes' and fun to the game, which makes AoS highly replayable. It's highly interactive, depending on the maps and # of players.

AoS is deep and unforgiving.  It requires careful planning for at least the next 2 turns; otherwise, one will have trouble in maintaining cash flow. Players have many tactical options. Building definite long-term strategies is slightly constrained due to the random goods assignment. Goods growth is the only random factor in AoS (Martin Wallace doesn't like perfect information game). Some players may find it too random to control; esp. when they are not good at making decisions based on partial information or calculating risks. 

AoS is a really good game. It's a pity that I can't bring it to table often because it's difficult to gather an appropriate group of players.

RATING: 8
After numerous plays, my assessment below still pretty much stands. However, I traded the game away nonetheless because it wasn't making it to the table. The game length always felt one or two rounds too long in a game that is already on the longer side. In addition, it doesn't handle lower player counts as well as some of my other economic games, which is important to me. Finally, as far as train games are concerned, this fit an odd middle ground between something like Chicago Express on the shorter end and 1830 on the long side. I'd still gladly play, but I don't really miss owning a copy. Such is the life of a man lucky enough to have a robust game collection.

---

After two plays on the Rust Belt map, I can see why this game is so highly regarded. Very tight. Unforgiving in a good way. I can see this becoming one of my favorites. The only downside is the runaway leader issues that some people dislike so much. Personally, even when I'm losing I tend to enjoy the game because I can always challenge myself with secondary objectives. It plays to my cutthroat gaming preferences I suppose.

Additional plays leave me wondering if this game doesn't go on for one or two turns too many. Even when the game is close, it does seem to take a while. Many times, though the winner is clear. Are those last two turns really necessary?
	
"Let's get the basics down: First, this game doesn't like you. It doesn't like anybody else either, but it particularly doesn't like you. If you ever have a question on the rules or how the rules apply to a particular situation then pick the answer that hurts the most. You'll almost certainly be right."

-- clearclaw


"[S]ome of my most memorable games [of Age of Steam] are the ones where I managed to hang on until the end without going bankrupt. I rode the death spiral and survived -- dead last place, but alive. The game itself is that much fun for me."

-- jdludlow

RATING: 9.5
What can I say? I like to ship things! But I also like the beginning of the game when things are so tight and tense and you're furrowing your brow double-checking your math to make sure your brilliant opening plan won't bankrupt you. And I like the middle of the game where you are racing to beat your fellow track-layers to your preferred goods. And I like the end of the game where your eyes are scurrying about the board trying to find that one 6-link delivery that will seal your victory. Oh, right, and I also love raucous auctions! And multiple strategy options! The only thing I don't like is those weird intersecting track tiles. Don't understand them, don't like them. Back in my day, we just built one straight track and blew up anything in its way, even the depot if we were feeling ornery!

RATING: 9.75
Tight economics and tense jockeying for position (both on the board and in the auction) make for an excellent game.  The multitude of expansions keep every game fresh - I don't think I've played any board more than twice yet.  Even with the same board, the cube distribution alters the landscape so significantly that I can't imagine two games feeling the same.  So much of the game depends on early choices that we always take our time at the outset to let people decide on their course of action.  I have the second edition, and prefer the wooden components and minimalist board and tile design.  We do play with clay poker chips, as the tokens that come with the game are rather cheesy.

RATING: 9
Hard to believe that I didn't play this until 2011 given my love of heavy economic games, in particular those involving trains. I had previously played Railroad Tycoon and its successors a handful of times, Steam once and the Early Railways once. I quickly saw this as the best of the lot for me. Wonderfully brutal, necessitating short-, medium- and long-term planning, and cutthroat.  This has all the elements of heavier games that I like. The huge number of expansion maps seem to offer almost unlimited variability among different numbers of players and small rules variations. I own perhaps a half dozen other maps, but haven't even had the opportunity to break them out yet. I'm really hoping to get more regular plays of this in 2012.

RATING: 10
Intense game.  My head hurts just thinking about it.  Challenging, interactive, alot of player control.  Decisons, decisions, decisions.  Interesting looking board to look at.  Who could ask for more?  The decision now is what expansion map to pick up.  Its actually really nice for the fact that a sixth player adds a considerable amount of chaos, which subtracts from downtime and necessity of skill.  The fact that the game can be a no luckbrain burner or a skill game with minimal luck depending on the number of players is fantastic.  Incredibly well designed, and scaled.  Thanks.

5 plays in, I feel like I am beginning to see the clever moves.  I am by no means an expert after five plays, but the game play has started to "slow down" and the amount of information and decisions are becoming quite managable.

UPDATE:  Yeah, its awesome.  Its the only game I can think of where I sit there and stare at the board looking for my best move instead of a good move.  Lots of fun. Lots of think.  Lots of railroad tracks.  I need to get up to speed... keep losing.

RATING: 10
Brutal, challenging, and completely unforgiving game... I can't wait to try it again! -- UPDATE: I've now had a chance to play Age of Steam 21 times, and I can say for certain that it deserves a solid 10.  It is undoubtedly one of the best board games ever designed.  The game presents players with countless decisions to make and the decisions are meaningful, which is really what I want out of a game.  However, it's not a particularly "friendly" game, so I suggest only playing with people who won't mind a pretty vicious game (Ticket to Ride this is not, by a long shot).  Finally, I should say that the additional maps available for this system make it even better.  I love how each of the other maps can completely change the game without forcing you to learn an entirely new ruleset.  You simply learn one or two rules changes and then you're ready to try Age of Steam on a totally new map.  I have a feeling that this will stretch the replayability of this game immeasurably.

I own the following 16 maps for Age of Steam: Rust Belt, England, Ireland, Western US, Germany, Scandanavia, Korea, France, Italy, Scotland, Alpha Centauri, Japan, Disco Inferno, Soul Train, Sun, and London.

UPDATE: I did not remotely understand how interesting this game was from reading the rules. It seemed like a whole lot of phases per turn, but it all just seemed to boil down to building track and shipping cubes to cities of the same color, which didn't sound all that interesting or compelling. I let this one sit on the shelf for much longer than it ever should have and I didn't think I'd ever need any expansion maps, but now over 20 games later and with 14 maps in my collection (paltry compared to some I know), I get it, I get why this one keeps people, including me, coming back and playing it again and again, and buying more and more maps. It's finally a eurogame that rewards long-term planning and not just short-term tactics, it's a game where you can have a completely new experience on a new map without having to learn a whole new set of rules, just a tweak here or there and you've got an entirely different experience, but 4 people who've played the base map before can just jump right in without any noticeable start-up time. The rules paradoxically make it seem both overly complicated with lots of phases and intricate rules for replacement/redireting but also too simple to hold my interest since it's just laying track and shipping cubes, but it turns out it's neither of those things in practice, but instead is one of the most interesting and engaging board games I've ever played.

Purchased: April 9, 2006

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The game is about building track to get irregularly appearing cubes from one city to a city of the same color as the cube. You make more the further the cube travels, ideally on your own track; you can use others' track as well but they get a corresponding cut. The deliveries you make pay from then on, not just one-time.

It feels like running a business: You have expenses to pay off but it may be hard to estimate how much money you will be able to bring in at a given time. If you can't pay your bills with cash, you must take cuts in income. It could result in a downward spiral.

I like this type of non-combat but map-based game where the particular map used makes a big difference, and players' actions on the map affect each other greatly, though perhaps in subtle ways.

...Some things I don't like: It's hard to incorporate the randomness of regenerating products [or rather, the complexity of the production situation] into my planning. Two, when delivering goods, you may have to stare at the board and count up rail links for a long time to figure out the indisputable best move. And three, the scoring doesn't take many things into consideration, making it tie-prone. Perhaps also the income reduction mechanic acts as a rubberbanding (catch-up) mechanism. (Some say its purpose is not to be a catch-up mechanism or even that it doesn't have that effect, but I disagree with the latter, as it does work towards eliminating distinctions among players' income. Taken to an extreme no one would ever pull ahead of anyone else.)

...Having played a few maps I'm upping to 8.0 and adding to my top 10. I like how each map feels like a new puzzle to figure out.

...I think in theory I don't care for Urbanization (player ability to create new cities on the map). I feel the effect of it is so great that it lessens the feel of distinctiveness of expansion maps. Also not really in love with how much analysis is required to understand the Production situation in a given game.

...I think this game is a brilliant example of incorporating long- vs. short-term considerations in a game. You know what you want your network to look like and that you need to race other players to get the best spots. But you also have to be mindful of what deliveries are actually currently available, so that you're not funding everything with loans.

RATING: 7
My impressions of Age of Steam have certainly been going through a bit of a rollercoaster.  First, I was really impressed.  But playing it a few more times, I grew disillusioned with the gamey-ness of certain parts of the game (in particular the cuts on the income track - I'd rather seen it split in more areas for a smoother progression from 0 to -8 or more - and the gamey effects of how payments for delivery work; which brings the effect that when taking a goods from a city to a neighbouring city, it's to my benefit to transport it via a long circuitous route).

Then, finding and playing 1825 made me see by comparison how tacked on the theme feels here.  Even though I had been negatively surprised when playing this before by the number of 'gamey' tactics that were needed, things that made no sense according to the theme of running a transportation business, but helped one perform well in the game, it wasn't until compared against the robustness of 18xx-games that Age of Steam really fell in my opinion.  Also more of a glaring annoyance in AoS when compared with the 18xx are the random elements, which even though small can still wreck carefully laid plans.

1825, on the other hand, it feels like things make much more sense, in the term of actually running railroad companies and investing in them.

As of lately, however, I've come to appreciate Age of Steam a bit more again.  Yes, it still tend to devolve into very gamey playing at times - but the early turns, struggling to make a profit, evoke an excellent feeling of being on the brink of financial ruin rare in economic games.  Some of the 18xx games match this by virtue of a harsh train rush... but apart from that, not many games come close as far as having to struggle against a tight economy to even survive goes.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Not the type of game I like, but such an excellent specimen of its type that I enjoy myself anyway.  I see why there are people who play this and nothing but this. Took me one play losing terribly before I was able to be competitive, but it was worth it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
 'Age of Steam' is similarly themed to that old Waddington's standby; 'Railroader' where players are American rail barons competing to lay down iron rails between cities and towns, to create the lengthiest, and therefore most profitable routes, over ten turns. They can use various card options, drawn in player turn order, that changes over each turn,according to the priority (one through seven) on the card used from the preceding turn. Like any new ganme, it will take a few plays to learn all the nuances, one of which is to maximise the number of links, short or otherwise, that allow you to ship goods along your own lines rather than somebody elses. You also need to watch out for building links across hills and rivers ad these cause your creditors to come a knockin' all too soon and can cost you victory points for going into debt. I would certainly play again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
The base game has some niggling issues, but the core ideas that have found themselves implemented into a plethora of variant maps are nothing short of brilliant. My favorite map is Montreal Metro, but there are other great maps, each with their own unique and interesting twists. The Age of Steam "universe" certainly has a place in the hall of fame of games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
GAME SYSTEM: This is, remains, and will always be a 10 for me.  After about 6 plays, I realized that I loved this game for many reasons, and I simply can't imagine ever not wanting to play this game.

Age of Steam gets a bad rap for being a heavy game, but it's not really that bad.  I think people see that the game is UNFORGIVING, and assume that it must be a heavy game, but the turn order makes sense, and flows logically from one point to the next.  There are certainly some angsty moments as the game attempts to squeeze the life out of you, and there are some incredibly tense moments as you attempt to out-think other players in what special action they may choose, and then wait to see what they're going to do.  Overall, though, this is probably better classed as a medium-weight game system, with the possibilities of certain expansion maps making the game that much harder.

Speaking of expansion maps, this is a large part of what made this game a 10 for me.  The base map is fine, and is a great introduction to the system, but that the system is versatile enough to allow for different adjustments and tweaks for different maps makes the game that much more appealing to me.  There are so many different maps, that provide so many different takes on the system, that it's hard to imagine the game ever getting old.  Even if you wind up playing the game on the base map, if it's been several months and several games since you played on it, the experience will still be new.

Furthermore, playing different games on the same map will show you how the system really adapts for replayability, because a previous game may have given the impression that a particular map was easy, when in reality it was the goods distribution that made it so.  Play the same map again, and you may find that the goods come out in such a way as to make it impossible to make a first turn delivery like you were hoping to do.

Versatile.  Replayable.  Challenging.  Crushing.  It's everything a game should be for me, and THAT is why it's a 10.

-----

RUST BELT: This is probably the best map to introduce new players to the game, just because it's the least quirky and the most accessible.  France is gentler, but not as challenging, and the Rust Belt doesn't have all the wild rules to wrap your head around like some of the later maps.  It also plays well with larger numbers, which is good, since many of the later maps work best at lower numbers.  That being said, though, I tend to play the expansion maps more than I do this one.

1 play: 3 players
6 plays: 4 players
4 plays: 5 players
Rating: 8

-----

SAHARA (prototype): This is my own design, so I like it.  As of 9-30-07, it still needs some tweaking.

2 plays: 3 players

RATING: 10
My favourite game. It's intense, no game plays the same. Every time I play the game I'm really impressed by it's sheer possibilities of choices. Understanding the game system isn't that hard, but to play it well is! To get the best out of your turns is a game on it's own; where to build, which goods to deliver or withhold, when to upgrade your engine, how many to bid for turn order for getting the role you need, should I sell more shares...choices, choices! It's often called a gamers game, but I really think people who like to be challenged will enjoy this game. If you only play to win, it's a gamers game... but if you like playing a game because it starts possessing your body and soul than AoS is a must buy. After each session I've played I feel like I've been in a gamers rollercoaster. Every game it astonishes me how inventive other players can be, making moves or builds I didn't see. The first sessions AoS played with me, after that I've learned to play with AoS. That's the moment where players recognize it's sheer brilliance! AoS is the only game I've bought twice (the new second edition), a truly 10!

RATING: 9
AoS is a great game with lots of interactions between players. The rules are super-simple despite its strategic depth. However, since (cooperative) kingmaker problem does exist, this game is far better when played among experts.

That said, the downside is that beginners can easily get knocked out. Also, first-turn moves, including starting position, is so important that it often determines the winner by the early stage of the game. Then, the rest of this LONG game is quite pointless, unless people care about the second place.

RATING: 7
The basic system is wonderful -- and that's why I love Steam.

But in AoS, if you make ONE mistake, you probably won't win. And if you make two, you'll probably get eliminated altogether.

Remember when we said that Euros were great because everyone kept playing until the end of the game (and everyone had a shot at victory, unless they really screwed up)?
Well, in that respect, AoS feels like a serious step backwards. 

People saying they prefer AoS over Steam *because* it's much more brutal and unforgiving sounds to me more like macho bullshit than anything else.
Take my first game: in turn 1, I made a $1 mistake, and it was immediately clear to everyone around the table (including me) that there was no way I was going to be able to recover from that mistake.
And that's supposed to be fun? 

To me, Steam's a 9. But I have to take one star away from AoS for its "make one mistake and you're dead" attitude, and a second star away for introducing randomness (goods cube distribution) in such a long-winded, calculatory game.

RATING: 7.5
A rail game that involves optimization of shipping goods from one city to another.  Very similar to Railroad Tycoon. Probably the trickiest thing is getting initial money from shares is not an instantaneous operation, hence AOS punishes a player more for bad planning.

RATING: 10
My #1 top-rated game.

OK, I'll say it.  I like games that are mean-spirited.

If there's ever a rules question in AoS, the rule should always be interpreted in such a way that it punishes players the most - and that will always be the correct interpretation.

In AoS, players are also given the opportunity to be mean to each other.  Repeatedly, but in a strategic way.  I like that.

The original version, without expansions, would probably get an 8 or 9.  But the original + entire line of expansions gets a 10, since I have a hard time separating one from the other.

RATING: 8.5
Played quite a lot (including plays of expansion maps). A rather demanding game, but well worth the effort in my opinion. It has interesting mechanisms (such as the auction system or the turn order with First build and First move actions) and the basic system is really adapted to expansions tweaking a few rules at a time. Unfortunately, the game might be a bit long (meaning there can be a runaway leader early enough, or on the contrary that an early mistake is likely to keep you out of the game for good). Also, the base map is not the most interesting one. I would rather suggest France or Ireland with 3 or 4 players, and England or Germany for 5 or 6 players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Initial comments circa 2006:  Tight and challenging game.  Not as brutal as I thought it would be given its reputation.  Plenty to think about.  Very engaging throughout.  No downtime.

July 5, 2012: Well, I've gotten 19 plays of it in, and I think I've got a pretty good sense of what the game has to offer and how to play well.  I've just decided that what it takes to play well isn't something that I find that fun.  While I like a lot of games in which you have to think hard, I don't enjoy the things that I have to think hard about in Age of Steam.  With the level of play in our group, it's become a very deep, cold calculation of route-planning and cube-seeking.  That's starting to seem too much like work.  So, I either can try to play optimally and not have that much fun or play sub-optimally and just enjoy the experience.  There are certainly games--Through the Ages comes to mind--that I enjoy for the experience even when I'm doing poorly, but Age of Steam isn't evocative enough to fall into that category.  So, while the different situations that Age of Steam and its various expansions offer are diverse, I don't find the central engine of the game engaging anymore.

RATING: 5.5
Very disappointed in the game. I've played very few games with more down time and less to do while you twiddle your thumbs waiting. The game rewards over analysis, and has no pace or cadence. Each turn can be analyzed to the Nth degree, and it boils down to mathematical projections and an ability to locate mathematically superior moves on the board. So rather than making clever or strategic plays, it's about spotting mathematics. I'm not good at these types of games, as I'm not willing to spend the time (ie, drag the game out) to map out every possible permutation of my turn actions. Horribly unforgiving game, as it seems like early mistakes can doom you from the start. That wouldn't be bad in a short game, but for such a long game, you could easily find yourself playing with no motivation for a long time. I can also see how one player feeling out of it could simply call it quits and declare a winner by shipping along another player's route, as the game features several elements which could be exploited for kingmaking. There are far many better games where I feel more involved and have more fun along the way. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9.5
Possibly the most notable and well received of all the pick up and deliver titles.  Age of Steam offers a rich gaming experience filled with frustration, angst and at times satisfaction (your mileage may vary).  It is one that rewards those that can plan ahead without being distracted by the immediate gratifications.  While the game is very unforgiving, I don't think it's extraordinarily difficult to perform well in a given session, especially with good money management.

Fairly steep learning curve, but I was proud that besides the opening move, I played the rest of the game without any assistance.  Kudos to a fantastic design, I am still thinking about strategy long after the game is over, totally eager to play again soon!
